J.T. Quite so. Israel became the head of the nations; they were this, we may say, during the reigns of David and Solomon, but through their failure they became the tail.
Rem. It is the assembly that holds Christ as supreme today.
J.T. That is what was reached in Ephesus. From our side the prayer is that we might know, "what the surpassing greatness of his power towards us who believe, according to the working of the might of his strength, in which he wrought in the Christ, in raising him from among the dead, and he set him down at his right hand in the heavenlies, above every principality, and authority and power, and dominion, and every name named, not only in this age, but also in that to come; and has put all things under his feet, and gave him to be the head over all things to the assembly, which is his body, the fulness of him who fills all in all" (@Ephesians 1: 19 - 23). It seems to me that in Joshua 11 we reach the climax of the conflict in the destruction of Hazor.
Ques. In connection with Joshua 8 and the reference to the apostle Paul going to Jerusalem as suggesting the descent into the valley, in Acts 23, when he reached Jerusalem and all the enemies were concentrated against him, and forty men had charged themselves with an oath till Paul was killed, a youth hears of it and saves the great vessel of the testimony.
{458}
Would that suggest the importance of the youth today, in the way of an ambush?
J.T. I am sure that is worthy of consideration, especially as the saints at Jerusalem, who yet occupied an official place, were lending no hand at all.
{459}
"THE POWER THAT WORKETH IN US"
@Ephesians 3: 20; @Isaiah 14: 7; @2 Samuel 23: 13 - 17
These scriptures speak of the power of God -- that is why I have read them, wishing to consider this great matter from the point of view of the first scripture, which says, "the power that worketh in us". There is power operative otherwise, creatorial power involving all the kingdoms, as we may speak of them, of creation, especially those involving life; the power of God is operative every day in giving life. In Him, it is said, indeed, that we all live and move and have our being; a great matter that is surely to be included in our faith. Indeed, the wide creation, far beyond, not merely our comprehension, but our apprehension, is dependent on the power of the Creator. "By faith we apprehend", we are told, "that the worlds were framed by the word of God" (@Hebrews 11: 3). They are poised and kept going, not as left to themselves, but by the power of God. Then there is governmental power operative all the time, especially since the placing of the cherubim in Eden, and, coming down to our own times, it is a matter of great consequence as holding the power of evil in check. We could not be here tonight, dear brethren, otherwise, and surely it is a matter to be held in our minds, in our believing minds. There is the good side to it; the powers are appointed for good, as it is said, "it is God's minister to thee for good" (@Romans 13:4). This could scarcely be so, aside from the discriminative adjusting power of God in government. The powers are forced to be good, for God is with government, in whomsoever's hands it may be. Our Lord will presently sit as a priest on His throne, and all will be regulated by Him according to His own heart. Today God has to operate through the bit and bridle, but nevertheless effectively, for the
{460}
horse remains a horse, and is forced to execute judgment, forced to execute government, by God.
I am thinking, however, as I said, of the power that worketh in us, the power that normally marks a believer from the very outset of his being, even before his birth, as seen in some. We have this inherent power, as I may call it, only in a relative sense in us, but nevertheless really so. We have it illustrated in a most remarkable way in unborn children -- the power of supplanting, for instance; in Jacob we have a striking illustration of what I am saying of the power of God. It may begin in a small way, but its potentialities are marked at the beginning. Here is an unborn child that will supplant one whom God will ultimately hate; he is doing something -- struggling -- and as they were born he took his brother by the heel to supplant him. So that we have in Jacob in result, one whom God loves; it is the operation of God's power.
So with another, whose name indeed gave me my text, who is spoken of as breaking forth, a great principle in the living kingdom, the kingdom of life, whether it be plant, animal, or saint -- the power of breaking forth, breaking through, so as to excel. In Pharez, (@Genesis 38) to whom I allude, we have one who broke forth, one who acquired his name thus, and who became famous in Israel. He is the progenitor of Boaz, of Obed, and of David. We have to look, dear brethren, at these initial movements. We have these examples, and they are examples of more than themselves. The creation as a testimony from God is full of those things, for it is a question of God; God working in us, working subjectively to accomplish results. So much is it so in the case of Pharez, that in the book of Ruth the genealogy begins with *him* instead of with Judah (@Ruth 4). He is owned to be Judah's son, but if God wishes to bring forth a worshipper, and the sweet psalmist of Israel,
{461}
He begins with Pharez. It is a question of potentialities in these initial movements of God. We see the power of life operating. On the one hand we have a man whom Jehovah loves -- "Jacob have I loved" -- his very name denoting that he had displaced somebody, and *that* somebody was hated of God; surely that is an exploit. To be in the mind of God in this way from one's beginning is surely something to be thought of, and to be nurtured and encouraged. So that we have, as I said, in Pharez, the progenitor of Obed, a worshipper -- the thought developed in David, the leader of the praises of Israel.
I speak of all this, dear brethren, that we might be on the look-out for these things; what God is doing according to the power that worketh in us. We have again in John the baptist a signal exemplification of what I am saying; as unborn he was capable of joy as the sound of the voice of the mother of Jesus fell upon his mother's ear. How great a thing that is, as suggesting how we are bound up together in the wonderful organism into which we are brought. With Jacob it was to supplant what God hated; with Pharez, to excel, and how could he excel more than in the development of the praises of Israel, the worship of God? It is the great thought for the moment that God is stressing amongst His people -- His service; and then the permeating joy as seen in the baptist. Its fulness was experienced as he heard the Bridegroom's voice. It is a question of Christ in relation to the object of His affection, and, dear brethren, we want to be sympathetic with Him in that; what He has secured at such infinite cost. John was the friend of the Bridegroom and rejoiced as he heard His voice. He had heard another voice, the voice of Mary coming to him through his mother; now he hears directly the voice of the Bridegroom and his
{462}
joy is full. He rejoiced with joy unspeakable, as we may say, and full of the glory. It is a question of glory, being filled with the glory of Christ as the Bridegroom -- "He that has the bride is the bridegroom". I speak of these evidences of the power that worketh in us as seen initially, so that we might be on the look-out for these evidences and nurture them. See how Rebecca looked after Jacob; she had the mind of God about him. See the care expended on Obed! See the divine pains taken so that he might come in as he did, and then the Spirit of God, as we may say, delighting in giving us the genealogy from Pharez down to the sweet psalmist of Israel.
Well now, this passage in Ephesians 3 -- confirmatory of all I have been saying, as I believe you will agree -- is the outcome of a prayer. It is one of the two prayers of Paul recorded in this epistle. The first, in chapter 1, related to the intelligence of the saints; that we might have the spirit of wisdom and revelation in the knowledge of God, and that we might know, among other things, "the surpassing greatness of his power towards us who believe, according to the working of the might of his strength, in which he wrought in the Christ in raising him from among the dead". In this second prayer, what is in mind is the Father's Spirit; the apostle is thinking of the Father, not now exactly as representing the dispensation of grace, but as naming the families; that there are families and that He names them all. Then that the Father would operate in the brethren; in the Ephesians, and of course in ourselves now, by His Spirit, and that it should be according to the riches of His glory. Think of the beautiful ornamentation surrounding this point that the apostle has in mind; the wealth of thought in which it is encased! It is like a jewel in a proper setting. The passage ends with a doxology. The
{463}
riches of the Father's glory -- think of that! and it is connected with His Spirit operating in us, that we might be strengthened with His Spirit in the inner man, and to the end that we should be rooted and founded in love. That is to say that this power, this unique sort of power, namely, the Father's Spirit, should operate in us, and that all the surroundings should be in keeping with it, that the saints should be *rooted and founded* in love. How great the need is to take care of the *roots* by which we draw the sap; and then the establishment, the founding, the equalising, so that we are steadily in the thing -- no longer babes! Then that the Christ, the great divine Operator, should be dwelling in our hearts by faith. What men are contemplated! So all the way down the chapter; "that ye may be fully able to apprehend with all the saints what is the breadth and length and depth and height; and to know the love of the Christ which surpasses knowledge". There are His infinite operations in the execution of the divine will; He is to be apprehended in faith in our hearts and held there, and we are to know His love in the midst of all that. He is not too much occupied with operations, dear brethren, to love us; nor should we be too occupied with operations to love Christ. We should take time for that surely, as He will take time to love us -- and He does. "To know the love of the Christ which surpasses knowledge; that ye may be filled even to all the fulness of God" -- meaning that we are in infinitude, and yet not lost in it; we are steadily filled in all these things, knowing the love of Christ and His personal thoughts of us, and ourselves filled; and if we are filled, it is to be with naught else but the fulness of God, nothing else, for God is to be all in all. It is a God known in Jesus -- a well-known God; no stranger, either He to us, or we to Him; there is no room for reminiscences there; eternal joy in the fulness
{464}
of God, and we filled with it. And so, as I said, it is "to him that is able to do far exceedingly above all which we ask or think, according to the power which works in us", power that we are already conscious of. God has been working in us from the very first spiritual movement we had, and it is by nothing else than this -- the power that works in us.
I now wish to go on to show how practically this power bursts forth, first in Isaiah. Isaiah is full, I may say, of this sort of thing. He contemplates the work of God in God's people, and the ultimate results according to it; not simply that the counsels of God will be fulfilled, but that the power that works in us will go through, and carry us through to finality. This verse in chapter 14 contemplates the fall of Babylon, a very significant fact of which Isaiah speaks exultingly. He is a prophet of great feeling, and feeling with God about things; so he puts into the mouth of the people of God a proverb against Babylon, now that she is doomed. The whole world had suffered from the king of Babylon, as he is spoken of here, and so the proverb runs, "The whole earth is at rest, is quiet: they break forth into singing". In our own times this is a very significant matter, one of the most significant of the public occurrences in the history of the assembly is the fall of Babylon. She will never be revived to what she was -- Babylon is fallen, is fallen, and has become the cage of every foul and hateful bird. But presently a greater calamity is coming -- her complete destruction; never to be revived. As a millstone is cast into the sea, so shall she be; not indeed lost to the judgment of God, but ever present to it, as it is said, "her smoke goes up to the ages of ages". Such has been her guilt that her judgment remains, stands out, as it were, eternally, and God would bring us to a judgment about all this. God says by
{465}
the Spirit, that He judges our judgment upon her; that is, the judgment of heaven and of the apostles *and* of the saints. If we work with God in our judgment, He works with us, He loves to do it. The judicial glories of God are very great, it is one of the features of His glory, and He calls us into it; to be with Him in what He does. As Babylon falls and is destroyed, heaven is called upon to praise, to rejoice -- and so it does; the mighty multitude of voices join in harmony, that God has executed His wrath on that great harlot, the corruptress of the earth.
Well, the result of all this, as you will see, is the release of what God seeks, namely, praise. Babylon has shackled the saints and she continues to do so; indeed all that is of her kind, in the religious world, also does so; it is intended by Satan to do it -- to prevent the praises; but they break forth; it is a question of power -- the power that works in us. It can be employed in no greater way than the breaking forth of divine praises. Will not God release His people? He *is* doing it. It is our business, too, to help Him, that God should have His praises. @Psalm 22, the great sorrow psalm, that which depicts the sufferings of Christ as none other does, exceeds in this matter. The cry of Christ there, "My God, my God, why hast thou forsaken me?" shows how He went to those depths in relation to the praises of Israel, the praises of God; as He says, "Thou art holy, thou that dwellest amid the praises of Israel". As come forth in resurrection, His word, as given to us by the Spirit in @Hebrews 2: 12, is, "In the midst of the assembly will I sing thy praises". There will be a universe of praise to God, through Christ, but it is now going on in the assembly; we are having part in it now; let everyone take it to heart, God is calling us into this great matter. So here, as Babylon is overthrown, the word is, "the whole earth is at rest, is quiet: they break forth into singing;" and
{466}
this has its counterpart in the book of Revelation, as I was remarking.
Finally, I wish to dwell on these three mighty men of David. We are brought back to John the baptist in his beautiful regard for Christ; how his joy was full at hearing the Bridegroom's voice. He was near enough to know what that voice meant; it is not simply the words, it is the voice of the Bridegroom. Let us understand, dear brethren, such a sound as that -- the voice of the Bridegroom, and how John is affected by it. He says, "my joy is fulfilled". A perfectly satisfied man, he disappears more than satisfied, his joy is fulfilled. Will not every one of us, as lovers of Christ, have a fulfilled joy in what we see in Him and in what He has in His *heart;* what He has in His bride; what the assembly is to Christ? I would like to come into that now, just to know what He thinks about the assembly, what He feels about it, what His affections are as to it.
Well, it is no question here with David of the bride, but something that his heart longed for, and the enemy stood between him and it. There are many such things, dear brethren -- the well at the gate of Bethlehem is well known; the Lord knows those that are His. He knows what joys He has had from some of them, but has no longer, alas! I often picture the Lord looking down on Palestine, Asia Minor, Greece and Italy, and North Africa, and countries nearer to us; what He must feel as He reflects on what He once had in those countries. He has not forgotten anything -- what Ephesus was and what joys He had there. He says later, "thou hast left thy first love" -- He missed that. There are many, living today, who yielded to Christ in times past, but the enemy has come in between Him and them. He is longing for the springing well -- for it is really the same thought -- springing up in affection to Him. "The water which I shall give him", says
{467}
Christ, "shall become in him a fountain of water, springing up ..." He looks for that. He is denied it by some here maybe -- certainly by many in the world -- the enemy has come in between. We know, for instance, in the case of a daughter of Abraham of old, the Spirit of God pictures her for us as being bent over -- Satan did that, he did it eighteen years before. The Lord straightened her, and she glorified God. The glorious things He is doing are spoken of in that section, and what are the glorious things He is doing today? Is there a saint here who loves Him who is not a testimony to the glorious things He is doing? "To us there is one God, the Father, of whom are all things, and *we* for him; and one Lord, Jesus Christ, by whom are all things, and *we* by him" (@1 Corinthians 8: 6). Is not every saint a miracle? It is a question of the works of Christ, the glorious things He did. One of them was to straighten that woman so that she might look up to God. Maybe there are some here whom Satan has ill-affected against God. Satan has come in between them and God, and robbed God of them.
My point here is that these three mighties broke through. They knew what David longed for. The assembly is the great thought of Christ for the moment, the one predominating thought throughout the dispensation. The gospel is subservient to it. The Lord has lost much through Satan's work, the enemy has come in, and it is a question now of breaking forth, laying down our lives, taking our lives in our hands, to get for Christ what He longs for. What does He long for? For the assembly; what He is robbed of in its members. So that certainly one might say to the brethren, our work is well defined; let nobody mistake it. The gospel, of course, is the work of the evangelist -- preach the word instant in season and out of season -- but it is all to get what Christ's heart is set for. We *know* what He wants -
{468}
most of us here do; He has made our work perfectly evident, dear brethren. "He that brings back a sinner from the error of his way shall save a soul from death and shall cover a multitude of sins" (@James 5: 20). What satanic power has to be broken through to get a soul for Christ! Indeed, the apostle says, "For this cause I endure all things for the sake of the elect, that *they* also may obtain the salvation which is in Christ Jesus" (@2 Timothy 2: 10). Certain ones -- they are in the mind of God, and they must be secured, and it is incumbent on us to get them; and these three mighties represent what is needed to get them.
That is all I have on my mind, and I leave the thoughts with you as to this question of breaking forth, the result of the power of God that works in every christian here and in every true believer on the earth.
{469}
ADMINISTRATIVE CONDITIONS
@Exodus 24: 3, 4; @1 Kings 18: 30 - 32; @Acts 26: 6, 7; @Revelation 7: 2 - 4
This wonderful period existing till now, from Pentecost, is called a dispensation; that is an economy, what is house-wise. I had in mind to show that it involves, at least in the attitude of God and those who are with Him, fellow workers with Him, a continuous dispensing of blessing; in a wider sense, administration. Many things that are included could be specified, but I mention just the general fact; having also in mind to call the attention of all to the divine thought that as one freely receives the benefits or any one benefit that is being dispensed, he himself is to become the dispenser of that, in so far as he may.
In @John 3: 35 we are told that the Father loves the Son, and that He has given all things to be in His hand. The next chapter proceeds to tell us that the Lord Jesus, at the well of Sychar, conversed with a woman, and opened up to her what was being dispensed, namely, the living water. Immediately, as she understands the Lord's mind, she becomes part of the administration. She had carried the vessel to draw water which was needed in her household administration, but now she is made acquainted with an infinitely greater thought as to water, and so she leaves her water-pot; her action intimating that she understood the Lord. She grasped in her soul something of what was in His mind. He said to her, "If thou knewest the gift of God, and who it is that says to thee, Give me to drink". That is to say, He took the attitude of receiving at her hands what she could dispense. He says, If thou knewest who it is that says, "Give me to drink, thou wouldest have asked of him" -- Thou wouldest regard Him in the light of
{470}
an Administrator, a Dispenser -- that is the lesson for every man and woman in the world to learn if they will. Those of us who are here have learnt it doubtless, through grace. What have we that we have *not* received? That is to say, all is on that principle. So the woman left her waterpot, meaning that she was now to dispense something greater than that which the Lord asked from her; she would be a dispenser of the great thing He was dispensing to her. She left her waterpot, and went her way into the city, and proceeded to administer, at least in the principle of her service. One has often mentioned that, but it is worth mentioning again, as illustrative of what is in mind, that the Lord is calling those who receive to become givers. "Give, and it shall be given to you" (@Luke 6: 38); "ye have received gratuitously, give gratuitously" (@Matthew 10: 8).
Now I go back to Genesis, because what is in mind, as you will observe, is the number twelve: the number of the tribes of the sons of Jacob, to whom the word of Jehovah came. Much had preceded Jacob -- accumulated wealth. Genesis treats of the wealth that was coming into the family of faith. Abraham was heir of the world: what immense wealth would be at his disposal! His heir was not to be Ishmael, although he, too, had twelve sons; the wealth of Abraham was not to be transmitted through these. Ishmael's hand was to be against every man's hand; a quarrelsome man is not the man through whom God would dispense the blessing which comes through faith. It is "faith working through love" (@Galatians 5: 6), and that did not characterise Ishmael. Hence Isaac was the heir; the rich inheritance of Abraham came down through Isaac; it was added to by Isaac, and it finally came into the hands of Jacob, the third patriarch. A great principle has been worked out in the three, as is always the case in such circumstances. The exercise is to be
{471}
completed in the third, and now in all that it means, it is to be diffused in twelve.
You will understand that I am not speaking literally, for the letter kills. We have to grasp the principle, that the great wealth of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob is to be dispensed. So immediately Jacob returns from Padan and has his name changed, the word of God came to him. Is there a soul in this audience who does not understand the greatness of the word of God coming to him? It is to enlighten our souls -- if it come and be received -- as to what is in the mind of God. The first one to whom the word of God is said to have come is Abraham; now it is Jacob. God is unfolding His mind to the patriarchs; they are to be initiated into what is in God's mind. What wealth there is, dear brethren! -- the riches of wisdom, of understanding, of truth, of knowledge: all that is, as it were, untapped; and it is let into the souls of those who have faith.
So the instructed believer, as Jacob evidently was, had in mind the twelve, and so did Rachel, for after the eleventh son is born she said, "Jehovah will add to me another son" (@Genesis 30: 24). So right through Genesis the thread runs. As Jacob departs, he speaks to his sons, and he constitutes them blessed. How necessary this is, for who can administer on God's behalf except one who is blessed, and blessed in all his faculties -- in his eyes, his ears, his hands, and his feet? "These are the twelve tribes of Israel, and this is what their father spoke to them; and he blessed them: every one according to his blessing he blessed them" (@Genesis 49: 28). So the line runs right through the books of the Pentateuch; indeed, it is worthy of note that the word "and" is the first word in each of the three books after Genesis. These early books begin with and, a small conjunction, meaning that something that preceded is carried down. And so with every one of us; the history of each believer
{472}
must begin with "and". Let each look back and see what that "and" means as to his own history.
Exodus takes up the thread and introduces us again to the twelve sons of Israel: "with Jacob had they come". Spiritually speaking he carried with him all the light of that which is so wonderful and rich, the inheritance of Abraham and Isaac, and now the accumulated wealth of the third patriarch. So the great administrative book immediately follows Genesis, and what I want to show, dear brethren, from the first scripture, is how Moses acted of himself in this matter. If one cannot do anything of himself, in the sense of which I am speaking, he is hardly in liberty of soul. The Lord loves to see us move in liberty. The history of Moses is written in large letters, in the initial part, at least, of the book of Exodus, and it is a history marked by the absorption of divine thoughts; it is not simply that one gets words, but that divine thoughts permeate him. After eighty years of such an experience in one way and another, he is now ready, according to the divine mind, to be affected by a divine appearing. Let us not think the word is too great to use, dear brethren. I think God is as pleased to appear to me, or to any of us, as he is ready for it, as He was to appear to Moses. No one really can represent God except as in some sense he has had an appearing. God would impress us in this way; He takes occasion to do it; He awaits His opportunity, as it were, and never fails when the time arrives; and it did arrive in Moses history, as he was about eighty years of age; forty years of which were spent in adverse circumstances, but it says, he was content in them. God loved that.
God had placed Moses in those circumstances in Midian, and Scripture says, "Moses consented to remain with the man" (@Exodus 2: 21 ), that is, with his father-in-law. He consented to remain with the man -
{473}
a different kind of man from what he had been, but still God had put him with this man, and he consented to remain with him. He is not complaining of his circumstances; he is not complaining, as it were, of the material, the personnel, of his local meeting. Where God has placed him, he is content, and God observes it, and says, so to speak, You will be content in more difficult circumstances too -- and he had to be. So God appears to him. We have to learn to be content in circumstances not quite to our liking. In all these conditions, dear brethren, God is making the man. He is working thus with every one of us as we let Him; for, indeed, it is a question of what we are *made*. The idea began with Adam: he was created, but being made is clearly a greater and more precious thought; it involves a personal relationship.
The line of Christ, as the Son of man, going back through Adam, is not through man viewed as created; that is not the thought. Adam is said to be "of God", in @Luke 3: 38; he was formed of God, and God breathed into his nostrils the breath of life; that is to say, there is a link; it is generation, not creation. The generation is greater than the creation. You cannot connect Christ with the creation, but you can with the generation. So that it is a question of our being made and breathed into. Adam was created, but he was made: "Jehovah Elohim formed Man" (@Genesis 2: 7). He is the handiwork of God not simply that he was created through the divine fiat, but formed by the hand of God. Think of the preciousness of that, and the link formed between Adam and Jehovah! Immediately, as some of us have often remarked, you get with Adam in that regard the divine name "Jehovah Elohim" -- all that God is in covenant relation, as we may say. He had entered into personal relations with Adam. So with Moses. He calls him twice, "Moses, Moses". Why
{474}
did He say that, dear brethren? Because Moses was interested in what he saw. I hope everyone hearing what I say is interested. God loves that; when He sees interested persons, then He takes notice of them peculiarly.
So He says, "Moses, Moses", and now He begins to introduce to him what he needed in his making. "The man Moses" is in mind -- one of the most outstanding men. The brethren who have been together here today have been speaking about Joshua; how he said to the sun, "Sun, stand still upon Gibeon; and thou, moon, in the valley of Ajalon! And the sun stood still, and the moon remained where it was ... . And there was no day like that before it or after it, that Jehovah hearkened to the voice of a man" (@Joshua 10: 12 - 14). It is a question of a man, dear brethren. "The man Moses" is a term that indicates distinction. We are told that there was no one like him in his day; "the form of Jehovah doth he behold" (@Numbers 12: 8). Think what impressions he would receive! Who can tell what Moses saw! -- What the form was! It all had in mind his own formation.
We find him, therefore, able to do things of himself. God did not tell him to build these twelve pillars. I want to point out, before going on to the next scripture, that these pillars are built consequent upon the avowed obedience of Israel, as if Moses were to say, Now that the people are ready to obey, we will have something: things will be done. I will not have to do everything myself. His father-in-law instructed him as to that. He had much to learn as to carrying it out; for our understanding often goes ahead of our feet, of our experience. He had to learn later that others could do the work too. The power was not increased, but the number of the workers was. He has in mind here that great things are now possible; for that is the point in Exodus -
{475}
the possibility of great things, dependent upon obedience.
Without obedience God can do nothing with us -- I mean, there can be no formation according to God without obedience. One must be pliable as the clay in the hands of the Potter. So Moses is now virtually saying, There are great things possible if we have an obedient Israel. If God has an obedient number of brethren in this town -- the twelve pillars are applicable; rear them at once; something is going to be done! It is no question of material things, but of this great moral element of obedience -- obedience to all the Lord said, *with no reserves*. Moses sees that something can be done now, and he rears up twelve pillars according to the twelve tribes of Israel. How fitting this is in this chapter, for the next chapter calls for something to be done; typically one of the greatest possible things was to be done, that is, to build a house for the Lord. "Make me a sanctuary", He says. God had made them obedient, as one might say -- they at least professed to be that; now Moses says virtually, We are going to have the administrative idea -- meaning that subject brethren will work together. Is the need not great, dear brethren, in every gathering represented here, of these twelve pillars? Of brethren in subjection working together? *Working together*, not simply sitting down together; there is that, too, but I am speaking now of God's operations, and of our being fellow-workmen with Him, called into His operations and working together in them. That is what Moses had in mind. It is a question of obedience, and I am speaking now particularly for young people here; for disobedience lurks strongly with young people. I am not accusing them; I know what it is in young people. Old people always have that advantage; as in the psalm David says, "I have been young, and now am old" (@Psalm 37: 25). A young
{476}
person cannot say, I have been old: he has that disadvantage. So that one who has been young knows what youth means.
Now Moses, in this section, immediately calls upon the youths to do the work on hand. Did they do it? Apparently well. They were young men. You know, the hope of Israel, if I might use that phrase, is young men and young women. Where can we look otherwise? We can look to God, of course. God can make stones to do the work of men, even to praise Him, but He is not doing that now. He is using us, though, in one sense, all that hard-heartedness is found with us, but He turns us; He changes us; He makes us to be what He needs in His operations, so that there can be this great thought of holding together; working together, without rivalry, each taking the lead in paying honour to the other. That is the twelve pillars. Moses instinctively understands that if this great people is ready to obey, then he can set up twelve pillars; and he immediately calls on the young men for service; that is to say, the idea is put into operation.
Now I need not remind you how often this thought of "twelve" appears in Exodus, and really it is always a question of the saints -- the shew bread, the twelve names on the breastplate of the high priest, and it is all founded on obedience. What pleasure could there be for God in having the loaves, "the bread of the presence", save as the underlying principle of obedience is there? What place has anyone morally in the breastplate of Jesus, save as the principle of obedience is there? Inside that breastplate, in His heart, is the law of God. He magnified the law, He made it honourable. Can He make it honourable by putting disobedient persons on that breastplate? No, dear brethren, the underlying principle in Exodus is obedience; the number
{477}
twelve loses its place without obedience. The number twelve has its distinctive place in Exodus.
I wish now to go on to Elijah. You will remember how these two men, Moses and Elijah, appear on the mountain with Jesus. What distinction they have! They are, along with David, the most outstanding men in the Old Testament, in the executive side of God's service as Paul and John are in the New Testament; so I selected those four men -- Moses, Elijah, Paul and John. Bring them together in your mind. Think of them in heaven! God's best from among men, as I might say. So Elijah follows up the service of Moses. Moses was used of God to assert His rights in obedience: Elijah is used of God to call God's people back to obedience. It is the same service. Paul says, "our whole twelve tribes serving incessantly day and night" -- what a thought he had! How could we have such a thought as Paul's, without Moses and Elijah? How could we have twelve tribes serving God incessantly day and night without those two ministries?
So Elijah comes in as a praying man; for, oh! what difficulties there are if young people, or the people of God generally, are disobedient! Precepts will not suffice, authority will not suffice; there must be prayer. Elijah prayed. He had in mind something that would bring back what had been lost -- Israel -- practically lost to God through the influence of Ahab and Jezebel. In Ahab's reign the high-handed act of Hiel the Bethelite in the rebuilding of Jericho had been perpetrated. What a day of rebellion, of disobedience! And think of our own times! Was there ever a day like ours? What can be done? Well, Elijah is the man: "he prayed with prayer" (@James 5: 17); and what that meant was, that there must be severe discipline; there must be severe deprivation, if the desired end is to be reached. This is a word for any disobedient person here, anyone
{478}
who has rejected divine principles. The Lord says, "He that has my commandments and keeps them, he it is that loves me" (@John 14: 21). If there be one or more in this audience who is in the class of those who are disobedient, professing to love the Lord Jesus, yet failing to keep His commandments, Elijah, if he were here, would pray. And what would he pray for? A drought. There are Elijahs in that sense -- mothers, fathers, brothers, sisters, husbands, wives, praying to God to bring about subjection. But let us remember, dear brethren, that if we pray for subjection, and discipline is necessary, we have to share in the discipline. If our prayers are to go through, we go through with them. We are not merely onlookers, we are not bystanders, who let God do the matter; no! Elijah has to go through with the discipline -- and he does.
Now he comes to the point to be reached, and he calls Israel to come near. How God would speak to some soul tonight! "Come near", he says: My first prayer about you was three years ago; now there is the sound of abundance of rain; the rain is coming -- no doubt about that. If you read his words here you read the words of a man who understands and knows, and has confidence in what he believes. He believes God, as Paul says, "I believe God that thus it shall be, as it has been said to me" (@Acts 27: 25). Elijah believed God; his words are firm, marked with certainty; they mark a man who knows, and believes God. He had an insight as to God, that perhaps few had before, or after him. His name would indicate that he had to do with Jah -- as we might say, the Absolute -- a man who could pray and wait for three years and go through the thing, is a man who knows God and is himself submissive to God. God says in effect to Elijah, I will go through with you and sustain you and maintain you on those lines.
{479}
And now the hour is come. He prayed again; he had prayed before, but he prayed again the second prayer, after he built this altar. The people are now in sight: obedience is coming. One often sees it in young people: they come to the meetings under protest sometimes. Presently they come without protest. Maybe they have been sitting at the very back of the room, but presently they begin to move forward: we see obedience coming. Elijah is a man who discerns what is coming: God is working; the drought is about to finish. He has met an Obadiah, a man who is in sympathy with him, the servant of the king. This man Obadiah thinks of the saints. What a comfort to Elijah must Obadiah have been! He hid a hundred men of Jehovah's prophets by fifty in a cave, he tells Elijah. He is really an assembly man, as one might say. God is giving Elijah inklings of what is coming. Presently, before there is a cloud in the sky, he says, "there is a sound of abundance of rain". Still he prays a second time. What confidence he would have in praying for that of which already he had heard the sound! And so it is, dear brethren, as we pray for subjection or recovery in our young, or in others. God would give us inklings that something is happening.
Now, he says, that something *is* happening, and he builds his altar with twelve stones, that is to say, the people were counted according to the twelve tribes -- "according to the number of the tribes of the sons of Jacob, to whom the word of Jehovah came saying, Israel shall be thy name". What encouragement in view of recovery, that the matter is now going on to what is spiritual! Recovery is of very little value in any one unless what is spiritual is in view, unless it comes about that in due course he surnames himself by "the name of Israel" (@Isaiah 44: 5). That being so, there is something to be done. Great things are coming. We must have them dispensed.
{480}
The victory is by one man, for it is a question of Elijah; as we read in James, who speaks of him as showing what one man can do; but what one man can do issues in that which benefits thousands -- millions indeed. But how is the benefit to reach them? It must be on this principle of administration according to God, not in those who make a trade of the word of God, not in hirelings, not in those instructed in priestcraft! This is a question of the sons of Jacob, whose name is Israel -- twelve of them. You see how this great servant Elijah, in conjunction with his fellow-servant Moses, is imbued with this great thought, that the great results of exercise, and of discipline, and of close dealings with God, must be dispensed. The whole assembly must get the benefit of it; it is not for one person, or for one locality, but for the whole assembly. If it be for the whole assembly, then rear up twelve stones for the altar, according to the number of the tribes of the sons of Jacob, to whom the word of God came.
Now I go on to Paul, but desirous of linking up these four great servants of God -- Moses, Elijah, Paul, and John. Paul here is in the presence of Agrippa. He is moved peculiarly. Anyone who reads the account, given by the Spirit, of his experience in the great valley of sorrow, into which he had come as arrested at Jerusalem, anyone who follows the history, would say that here he is full of holy feeling. One of the most important things in a minister is, that he should be a feeling man, one capable of exclamations in dealing with the things of God. It is constant with Paul, but peculiarly so in the remarkable address which is recorded in this chapter. He is speaking to king Agrippa, and he is speaking about the promises, about the hope, as he says, "And now I stand to be judged because of the hope of the promise made by God to our fathers, to which our whole twelve tribes serving incessantly
{481}
day and night hope to arrive". He is in the light of the promises, and what wealth is involved in the promises! In taking them up, we become energetic in the service of God. "Having therefore these promises, beloved" (@2 Corinthians 7: 1), the apostle says; and "That by two unchangeable things, in which it was impossible that God should lie, we might have a strong encouragement" (@Hebrews 6: 18). God loves us to take up His promises, and the apostle is led to say here, in dealing with this great matter of promise and of hope, "of the promise ... to which our whole twelve tribes serving incessantly day and night hope to arrive". -- "Our whole twelve tribes" -- he is enriched in having them.
May we not use the same words, because the number twelve as applying to the tribes is a question of a principle; that is to say, the saints serving together, and serving instantly; not in any tardy or perfunctory way, but "serving incessantly day and night"? You might say, Could they be found? Well, I do not think the apostle would say this were it otherwise. There was much more at the beginning of this dispensation than is recorded. Think of the five hundred brethren to whom the Lord appeared at one time! What a movement of heart there would be towards Him as He appeared to them! How could we fail to serve such a Saviour? How could we fail to be energetic on the side of such a glorious Head as He? Think of the impression made on their souls by this appearing! And the apostle says, "of whom the most remain until now" (@1 Corinthians 15: 6). I suppose they were known as a class; it would be said, That is one of the five hundred. Possibly the apostle could pick out twelve of those. You say, Well, the ten tribes are lost -- the Spirit of God does not speak thus, and He knows better than we. There is no such thing in Scripture as *the lost ten tribes*. No! that is human thought; faith does not speak thus.
{482}
Faith says, "Our whole twelve tribes serving incessantly day and night hope to arrive".
There are those who deal in prophecy and yet are linked with the world, if not in a bad sense, as we say, certainly they are in a religious sense. They are dealing with prophecy academically, as a subject for the mind; but the twelve tribes of Israel can only be apprehended by faith; they have promises and they have hope. If I believe God, I accept His promises; they will never fail. They are a strong consolation, and I am full of hope. James says, If you talk about your faith and your hope, I want to see your works; and the word he uses as to Abraham is, that Abraham was justified by works, and so was Rahab -- a remarkable pair to be put together, both justified by works! Abraham was isolated in his day and so was Rahab, but she was justified by works, even as was Abraham; and so are "our whole twelve tribes", as the apostle says. He does not say, the number of the sons of Jacob; he says, "our whole twelve tribes". Let us look round, dear brethren, and see if we do not know some of them. They are justified by works, full of faith and hope, but serving God incessantly day and night, and to God's promise they hope to arrive. They are like Anna, who, we are told, departed not from the temple, but served God with fastings and prayers night and day, speaking of the Lord to all those who looked for redemption in Jerusalem.
Finally, just a word about John, the beloved apostle, now the beloved prophet. In a way, one wonders in which role he is most interesting. Is he more lovable as an apostle than as a prophet? Or perhaps I should add, as a brother? I believe he is more lovable as a brother than as in any other relation. "Your brother", he says, "and fellow-partaker in the tribulation and kingdom and patience, in Jesus" (@Revelation 1: 9). What a brother he was! And
{483}
who can be an apostle or a prophet in the true sense, unless he is a brother? No one. Think of "five hundred brethren at once"! One of the finest words in the divine vocabulary is the word "brethren" -- not in the hackneyed sense in which it is used around us, but in the divine sense.
I am not saying John acted of himself here: he is telling something he saw. I think it is incumbent upon one to mention him now, because he brings in the number twelve more than any. This remarkable book, the Apocalypse, is replete with the idea, especially in relation to that of which we all form part. I hope there is no one here who does not form part of the assembly, which is about to be revealed as the heavenly city! We must understand this number twelve; it is everywhere in the heavenly city, both in regard to the measurements and to the persons; the most precious things are all governed by this number twelve, and what is it, dear brethren, but the excess of love? The beloved disciple understands it, and he puts into this record, which he gives us in the last book, the intensification of the idea. Who knew love better than John? He says, "Hereby we have known love" (@1 John 3: 16). He knew it. Where did he learn it? Where only it can be learnt, dear brethren, in the bosom of Jesus; that is where love is; all divine love is there; and really there is no love otherwise. The meaning of the love was not known until Jesus made it manifest; and John has it in a first-hand way; he has it, and he learned it in the bosom of Jesus. There is the objective attestation of love, as he says, "he has laid down his life for us". Every christian can say that, but not every christian can say, I know love as having learnt it in the bosom of Jesus.
John in Revelation enlarges on this great thought of twelve, or its multiple, whether here, in chapter 7, in the one hundred and forty-four thousand, or in
{484}
the many references to it in the heavenly city. I only touch on that at the finish so that you may have the greatness of it in mind. How wonderful will be the dispensation to come! At least as far as the heavenly city has part in it. It is all a question of *perfect love*, not a shade of rivalry or personal feeling -- all will be perfect love, and in so far as it is reached, there is what is final; for perfect love refers to what is final. What a wonderful time is ahead of us, in having part in that which is marked by perfect love in activity! Is it not incumbent upon us to look into this matter, and see whether the little we have received is being handled properly -- enjoyed in our souls and then handed on to others?
The youngest believer is called to have part in this great matter, and to learn how to do things without partiality, without hypocrisy, without thought of personal distinction, but in perfect love "faith working through love".
May God bless these thoughts to all of us.
{485}
THE GREATNESS OF THE SAINTS
@1 Corinthians 3: 21 - 23; @2 Corinthians 4: 5; @Deuteronomy 33: 29; @2 Samuel 7: 23, 24
J.T. What is in mind is that we might dwell a little on the relation of the servants of the Lord to those ministered to. The first two scriptures are expressions of the apostle Paul, the leading minister of Christ, showing how he regarded the saints, even when they were in a low condition. In the second epistle he was a servant to the Corinthians "for Jesus sake". It seems that in view of what is about to be unveiled -- that is, the saints in their millennial and eternal relations -- the Lord would lay it upon us that they should be rightly regarded as we serve them. In @Revelation 3: 9 the Lord intimates that the saints, as in His mind (inclusive of all regarded as of the assembly, whilst, of course, it is remnant times) are to be done homage to in whatever manner He may bring it about. He says, "Behold, I make them of the synagogue of Satan who say that they are Jews, and are not, but lie; behold, I will cause that they shall come and shall do homage before thy feet, and shall know that *I* have loved thee". It is evidently in view of what the Revelation opens up to us in chapter 21, the assembly in its eternal and millennial relations, that the Spirit greatly stresses its greatness. I thought also that it would augment the truth to bring forward what the two great ministers of the Old Testament -- Moses and David -- say of the saints, in the passages read.
The fact that the apostle brings this thought into his letters to the Corinthians, is to show us by the Spirit, that in order to regard the saints in the light of their true greatness we must not be beclouded by any low conditions that may exist. Low as the conditions were at Corinth, the apostle says to them,
{486}
"for all things are yours. Whether Paul, or Apollos, or Cephas, or the world, or life, or death, or things present, or things coming, all are yours; and *ye* are Christ's, and Christ is God's" (@1 Corinthians 3: 21 - 23). He does not speak abstractly, but to them, to those very saints that he describes in the first verse as not spiritual, saying, "And *I*, brethren, have not been able to speak to you as to spiritual". The second epistle is more applicable to himself, as to what he was: "For we do not preach ourselves, but Christ Jesus Lord, and ourselves your bondmen for Jesus sake" (@1 Corinthians 4: 5). And then he says, "For all things are for your sakes" (@2 Corinthians 4: 15).
I think the most practical and profitable way would be to consider the circumstances in which the apostle utters these remarkable statements, so that in serving the saints, those who do serve in any special way, may have right thoughts, may be in a right attitude of mind -- "ourselves your bondmen for Jesus sake". The servant is among the "all things" that belong to the saints, and one might venture to add practically that consideration of this matter will deter us from exploiting the saints in any way. They become discredited and lose their character and dignity in our minds, if we exploit them in any way, however small, whether commercially, professionally, or ministerially. The thought of bondmen places us in the lowly attitude that is becoming, in view of the greatness of the persons we are serving. It says in @Hebrews 7: 26, "For such a high priest became us". The Lord of glory, the Creator, is presented to us in priesthood, and we are told that such a great personage as He was necessary to be our Priest.
E.G. You have often used the expression, Clothe the saints with the thoughts of God. Does this fit in with what you have in mind?
J.T. It does. The saints may find it difficult to
{487}
accept the great thoughts which Scripture attaches to them, and it is incumbent on those who do know, who accept it and appreciate it, to convey in their attitude towards the saints how great they are. Not of course in the flesh, for the apostle deals with them most severely from that point of view, but in his mind they are the owners of everything.
W.S.S. Would that be involved in Solomon speaking of himself as a little child when viewing the people of God?
J.T. "So great a people!" How he clothed them! @Luke 15: 22 says, "Bring out the best robe and clothe him in it". It is for us to convey in our attitude towards the saints this impression; of ourselves, "your bondmen for Jesus sake". Yet not sparing the flesh in the brethren, because they are not to be exalted on *that* line at all. These epistles are most severe in dealing with that side of the truth.
Ques. Do we see the way the Lord would clothe the saints in the last chapter of Matthew? He says, "Hail!" Is that a salutation of respect, recognising the subjective work which was represented there?
J.T. I think that is right. The apostle's salutations to those at Rome fit in, but they are to greet one another with a *holy* kiss.
A.M.H. @1 Corinthians 1: 30 says, "of him are *ye* in Christ Jesus". Is that viewing what the saints are initially?
J.T. Yes. They are of God in Christ Jesus, "who has been made to us wisdom from God, and righteousness, and holiness, and redemption". I suppose such a statement is needed in an epistle like this, because the work of God at Corinth had become obscure and shallow, but still, they are in Christ; they have that status. Although babes, the apostle would not have any other thought. If leaven is there, it is to be purged out "that ye may be a new lump, according as ye are unleavened;" he keeps that abstract
{488}
thought in mind. We should not take up any other attitude in regard to the saints, however low their state, for a principle of service is to carry the saints along with us, not to travel too fast, or to lord it over God's heritage. So that Moses and Aaron, seen in @Exodus 4, are typical ministers; they are in their initial service, and the people worship because of what they do. Then when the instructions are given as to the passover, the people again worship as the minister serves them (@Exodus 12: 27). Further, in @Exodus 16: 10 as the people would have gone back to Egypt, it says, "when Aaron spoke to the whole assembly of the children of Israel, that they turned toward the wilderness ... ;" showing that influence for good normally goes with the ministry.
Ques. Would this tend to improve the quality of what is ministered?
J.T. I think so; the bondman is more likely to minister Christ, who is supremely the Bondman. He will be more likely to present Christ aright than any who in any way lord it over the saints.
S.B. Would it be right to say that David has these qualities when he speaks of the Philistines defying the armies of the living God, and challenges Goliath himself "in the name of Jehovah of hosts, the God of the armies of Israel, whom thou hast defied" (@1 Samuel 17: 45). Is that the reason Jehovah later spoke of choosing David to feed His people?
J.T. Just so. One is impressed with the way David qualifies, especially in the pressure of the battle in the valley of Elah; his very beauties are alluded to. And then in answer to Eliab he said there was "a cause;" he was under orders, for his father had sent him, and he is so full of the thought of the armies of Israel and who they were, that for such a one as Goliath, great as he may have seemed to the natural mind, to have defied them, was abhorrent to him. What a place the armies of Israel
{489}
had with him, the armies of the living God! And so, completing his service, he is peculiarly lowly when speaking to Saul. Saul was very supercilious even after the victory, when the giant's head was in David's hand; but David says, "I am the son of thy servant Jesse the Bethlehemite". Then it goes on to say, "when he had ended speaking to Saul, that the soul of Jonathan was knit with the soul of David, and Jonathan loved him as his own soul" (@1 Samuel 18: 1). He stripped himself to clothe David; he clothed him with the greatness which belonged to himself at least. Perhaps he did not go quite far enough, but he went a good way. This all shows the spirit of the servant, and how he attracts those he serves, for he had served Jonathan and Saul wonderfully at that moment; after that we get the maidens of Israel coming out and sounding David's praises.
W.J.T. You spoke of the servant carrying the saints along with him. Is that beautifully illustrated in @Numbers 11: 12, "thou sayest to me, Carry them in thy bosom"?
J.T. That in itself is very beautiful, although in the stress of circumstances spoken in unbelief; it corresponds with our own great apostle's remarks to the Thessalonians, when speaking of being in the midst of them as a nurse cherishing her own children. The Thessalonians were just growing babes, but Paul clothes them with parental affection.
Ques. Would you say that wisdom is called for in the particular aspect of the truth to be presented at any given time? Here they were quarrelling between themselves as to leadership, and this brings out the great thought in chapter 3. Then in chapter 6 we get another choice thought, "he that is joined to the Lord is one Spirit". Should it be known to the minister which feature of the truth should be presented on each particular occasion?
J.T. Yes. Here he was transferring the thoughts
{490}
of party leaders to himself and Apollos for their sakes, and brings in this great thought which is very remarkable. We have spoken elsewhere of Joshua speaking to Jehovah, and calling upon the sun and moon to stand still. The two great orbs belong to the saints. What an elevating thought is this, even as to christians in a low state, babes -- the sun and the moon belong to them! In royal families they follow this principle of keeping the children nourished with high thoughts -- they are, of course, on natural lines, but that is the principle; the saints should be nourished with great thoughts, whilst carefully instructed as to the utter worthlessness of the flesh. It is a great matter that the servant is able to bring the great thoughts of God forward, helping to lift us out of the littleness of the flesh.
J.T.S. Would the spirit of this be seen in @Exodus 33: 13, 16? Moses says, "and consider that this nation is thy people", and then he says, "And how shall it be known then that I have found grace in thine eyes -- I and thy people?" In the next chapter he says, "Let the Lord, I pray thee, go in our midst; for it is a stiff-necked people; and pardon our iniquity and our sin". He would not allow the flesh notwithstanding the greatness of the people?
J.T. That is very good. I had thought of that passage, because Moses is the outstanding minister of the Old Testament along with David. God must have given Moses a wonderful impression of the people -- much more than is recorded. In returning from Midian the circumcision was enforced. Jehovah was going to slay Moses, showing how He would impress him with the utter incorrigibleness of the flesh and its uselessness. His wife says he is "A bloody husband", because of the circumcision. So he must have had a great conception of the utter uselessness of the flesh in whatever way he may have understood it. It might be inquired, Why "I
{491}
and thy people"? Paul would have said, Thy people and I. Evidently when the saints have gone astray and have come under fleshly influences, it is necessary that the authority of the minister should be impressed upon them; for, in truth, he is representative of Christ. He is not less, because he is a bondman; indeed, Jesus was a Bondman. If a man becomes a bondman, God does not fail to exalt that man. In Moses case it was peculiarly so. God will not have His servant ill-treated, He will insist upon his greatness. The tabernacle was pitched outside and God appeared to Moses and spoke to him as a man speaks with his friend; all that his dignity might be known and that he should have influence with the people. When Moses is about to die, the point that he has before him is the greatness of the people. After all, what is the value of any service save as it promotes the greatness of God's people? The greater they are spiritually, the greater is God's inheritance in them.
M.W. B. If we are to serve rightly, we must always bear in mind the greatness of the people as divinely constituted, and yet have a sober regard to their practical state; prepared to take up the feelings proper to the displeasure of God on account of that state.
J.T. Quite so -- "having in readiness to avenge all disobedience". The true servant is prepared for all contingencies.
J.J. Do you have that view of things when Balaam speaks in Numbers 23 and 24? Does it bring out the greatness of the people?
J.T. Yes. I suppose the divine thought was to augment what was already there in view of the people entering the land. Moses, as representing authority, could not take them in, but as a man of God he clothes them with wonderful thoughts. Moses had nothing to do with Balaam's prophecies; they
{492}
were all the direct action of God, showing how He comes in sovereignly to augment the ministry; we can always expect that. Whatever God is stressing in ministry He will come in Himself to augment it with something, so that it might be enforced. He brings out the greatness of the people in those remarkable prophecies, or, as we may call them, divinely regulated pronouncements. Moses undoubtedly wrote the account. How it would enlarge his soul as to God acting to support him; and then you get the numbering, as much as to say, Such people as this can be numbered for the heavenly land.
A.T. Does the Lord's supper help in this matter?
J.T. It is introduced in this epistle under the words, "I speak as to intelligent persons". A feature of the assembly is that it is composed of "intelligent" persons; a thought which should be with us always in sitting down to the Lord's supper in assembly, Thus we are "one body".
P.L. Would the greatness of the saints in the Lord's mind be seen in the way He speaks to the Father as to them in @John 17?
J.T. We have referred to that elsewhere -- how the Lord laboured, according to John, to impress them with the Father, and who they were, too, in the Father's mind. Then finally the Lord speaks of them not as babes, but as *men*. He had spoken to His Father about them as babes, in @Matthew 11, which is very touching. Now He speaks to His Father about them as men, and asks for such things as would enhance their greatness; their sanctification would be like His; they would be with Him; the glory He had received He would give them, He would glorify them with it; and they would have the kind of love the Father had. What men they would be, able to love Christ as the Father loves Him!
F.S.M. Is it not characteristic of the writers of the epistles that they approach their task with the
{493}
consciousness of the dignity of the saints? In Colossians, it is "to the holy and faithful brethren in Christ;" in Hebrews, "holy brethren, partakers of the heavenly calling;" in 1 Peter, "to the ... elect according to the foreknowledge of God the Father". If we are impressed with the dignity of the saints and have a sense of what God thinks about them, would that not give effect to our service throughout?
J.T. Just so. We should have this before us more -- *to whom* we are ministering. In @Luke 10: 20, when the ministers had just come back, having done well in their service, the Lord says to them, "in this rejoice not, that the spirits are subjected to you, but rejoice that your names are written in the heavens". It is one of the most beautiful touches in Luke. They were not to rejoice because of their success in service, but that their names were written in the heavens. That gives dignity -- it is their status up there. What a glorious prospect that is! Viewed thus, how worthy the saints are of service!
Ques. Does @Numbers 20: 10 show the importance of viewing the saints rightly? Moses says, "Ye rebels", but Jehovah viewed them very differently.
J.T. It is very important for servants to bear this in mind, for we all know how irritated we become. This great servant, in that sorrowful time, called the people rebels. You cannot go into the land with such thoughts of bitter resentment, because the brethren will not listen. One knows well enough, as no doubt many do, how these thoughts arise, and this is written to check us. God will not brook it, He will not have the saints viewed otherwise than in relation to His counsels and His work in them. On the other hand, as those, having the place of God's people, turn away from the truth, become opposers of it, reprobate, the strongest condemnatory terms are used in Scripture of them. The Lord Himself speaks of a wicked and adulterous generation, and
{494}
Stephen says, "O stiffnecked and uncircumcised in heart and ears, *ye* do always resist the Holy Spirit; as your fathers, *ye* also" (@Acts 7: 51).
A.F.M. Jehovah defended Moses when Miriam and Aaron spoke against him.
J.T. We have just been remarking as to Moses saying, "I and thy people". The mediator must be distinguished, and the honour due to every servant is divinely maintained. Moses, in placing himself first, no doubt rises to the thought of Christ. He says, "so shall we be distinguished, I and thy people". It is beautiful language and evidently God was greatly pleased with it.
Ques. Do you get a further example of what you are saying in @Luke 22: 27? When the disciples were questioning amongst themselves as to who should be the greatest, the Lord says, "*I* am in the midst of you as the one that serves", and then He goes on to speak to them of having appointed unto them a kingdom.
J.T. That is very remarkable; and they were to sit on thrones judging the twelve tribes of Israel. The thought is to lift the saints out of their pettiness and rivalry. To bring in their greatness, what they are in the mind of God, is part of the service of the minister, and he is to serve as a bondman for Jesus sake.
E.J.McB. Viewing the greatness of the saints as God looks at them, the servant would seek to lift them out of the conditions in which they are, so that they may touch the thoughts of God for them.
J.T. That is what we get in 2 Corinthians, where we have correspondence with Moses. In @Exodus 34 Moses goes up the second time, and it is left to him to hew the stones, and God would write on them. Then we get the reference to the fact that the skin of his face shone. It is the new covenant *figuratively*, and God furnishes the minister with the means to
{495}
lead the saints on. He adds sovereignly to what the minister is aiming at, which is very comforting, for God is doing this all the time. Sometimes we really wonder what can be said next; so much ground has been covered. The Spirit of God has infinite resources, but the servant has to keep applying death to himself so as to be available in the dispensing of them. Joshua had to go down into the valley, as we have been seeing recently; he gets into the position of Christ, where Christ was. In Moses case God furnishes him with fresh impressions, for God was not giving up. Moses has right thoughts, as we see in @Exodus 33; he was thoroughly with God in what He had before Him. Jehovah passed before Moses "and proclaimed, Jehovah, Jehovah God merciful and gracious" -- a remarkable thing. The servant has to be ready to be adjusted all the time as to himself -- old things will not do. One often has felt like Samson; he found, we are told, the fresh jaw-bone of an ass -- an unlikely thing, from the human point of view, but it was just what was needed. The apostle Paul was in great sorrow as to the Corinthians, but he says, as it were, 1 am going on with *you*. I cannot write another letter to you like the first; and so he says, "Now he that establishes us with you in Christ, and has anointed us, is God". Note "with *you*" -- he is with the brethren and they are now, we may say, with him. What will he say next? It is remarkable that the matter of the letter of commendation comes in just at this point; a very small thing in itself, but wonderful things open out from it. So now in the midst of the greatest things, the richest things to affect the brethren who needed recovery and establishing in the truth, we get the new covenant.
S.B. Would it be right to say that the defection of the people at Kadesh-barnea would not have occurred had they risen to the height of God's
{496}
thought for them? Could Caleb and Joshua be referred to as fit to lead the people on?
J.T. Yes. It is an immense thing to hold the ground in a crisis, whether it be by many or few. Something is bound to happen; the enemy is not going to gain the day if two men, or even one man, is prepared to hold the ground.
J.J. Do we get the authority side in the first epistle and the priestly side in the second epistle to Corinthians?
J.T. We get the priestly side in both epistles, but the first is more what Moses is typical of. The Aaronic character comes in more in the second epistle; but *authority* is stressed in both. In the second the apostle says, "Having in readiness to avenge all disobedience". But in chapter 3 he is dealing with them in the Spirit of Christ as the Mediator of the covenant; for that is the point. They went so far as to suggest that he should have a letter of commendation, and this gave him the "fresh jawbone of an ass", so to speak, that is chapter 3. It was there; I do not think it was premeditated, and the Lord was with him in it. The parenthesis, verses 7 - 16, was not premeditated, but what a wonderful opening up of the covenant it was! What a weapon the apostle had -- what a means of leading the brethren on with him! I believe the servant will always find that, if he has the true thought of God as to the greatness of the saints whom he is serving. The Lord will furnish us with what is needed. We must not go too far ahead, but seek to bring them along the line that we ourselves are on.
Ques. Would the servant be very greatly cast upon God in his ministry to be able to handle things like this, and make way for the expression of the truth needed?
J.T. Quite so. Here it is a letter of commendation, which is a small matter, but God makes it a
{497}
very great matter; for it is a means of helping the brethren at that particular juncture. He follows on in chapter 3, and speaks of the covenant by itself, and this, as we know, fits in with the Lord's supper. Then he alludes to bondmen, that is, himself in relation to the Corinthians. Chapter 4 begins, "Therefore, having this ministry ..." He is going on with them in the midst of such rich thoughts as the thought of a letter of commendation has led to.
H.H. I suppose that Moses blessing the tribes would correspond with the letter of commendation that Paul speaks about. Moses spoke *well* of Israel in @Deuteronomy 33.
J.T. Yes. Anyone meeting Moses at that time and asking about Israel would hear him speak well of them; he would not call them rebels. The whole chapter is a wonderful testimony to what Moses thought of Israel; he does not go quite so far as to say that he loved the tribes, but he says that God does, and we may see how thoroughly he is with that; indeed, he is "the man of God" in blessing Israel.
W.S.S. Does this come out in @Daniel 9: 18, 19? He says to God, "behold our desolations, and the city that is called by thy name", and then, "thy people are called by thy name".
J.T. Just so. There is a great destiny for that people, and all would be fulfilled. The man clothed in linen says (@Daniel 12: 9 - 13), "Go thy way, Daniel ... thou shalt rest, and stand in thy lot at the end of the days". What a beautiful word for the servant.
A.P.T. Is @Romans 16 something further than the jaw-bone of an ass? In finishing, the apostle brings in Phoebe and her relations with the saints.
J.T. Yes. Phoebe is a minister of the assembly and she is spoken of as possessing dignified qualities. She is evidently the bearer of the letter to the Romans, and the apostle's commendation of her is
{498}
included. This brings up the question of letters of commendation, because sometimes a letter is a little out of keeping with the person who is bearing it. Paul in writing his letter of commendation of Phoebe speaks also of others, as if it furnished him with what was needed in the finishing of his letter. He evidently knew much about the persons he saluted, and his salutations would serve a good purpose, when commending this dear sister living near by. He had affectionate regard for the saints at Rome, and a specific estimation of some of them, and he takes advantage of the opportunity offered to give expression to it. It is necessary that we should have a right estimation of the brethren we meet. We are to hold them in our minds as God regards them. The apostle's salutations would strengthen the position, because through them the assembly at Rome would be in its own account greater. The letter of commendation would suggest the *work of God* in persons so noted; and of course there were other persons there equally the subjects of the work of God.
J.S.E. Does not Paul, in the way he covers the saints in the first letter to Corinth, help them forward in the direction of the thoughts of God to such an extent, that they themselves could refuse the things that damaged them? They would now regard them as beneath them.
J.T. That is suggestive of what opens up so wonderfully in chapters 3 and 4 of the second epistle. "Therefore, having this ministry, as we have had mercy shewn us, we faint not". As if the new covenant came into his mind and he was greatly stimulated. "But we have rejected the hidden things of shame" and "we have this treasure in earthen vessels, that the surpassingness of the power may be of God, and not from us". He now allows what he is speaking of its own weight in his own soul, clothing his own position with the great thought of the new
{499}
covenant, and he says, "your bondmen for Jesus sake". Surely this was to the end that they might take it on and clothe themselves with it, too. Then he goes on to say, "while we look not at the things that are seen, but at the things that are not seen; for the things that are seen are for a time, but those that are not seen eternal". He would link that on with the thought of the new covenant.
P.H.H. Is that seen in @Exodus 34, where the skin of Moses face shone, but he did not know it? Would it be what you are speaking of -- something furnished by God in order to bring the people into what Moses had?
J.T. Yes; it would enhance His servant. They were afraid of him, and it would seem as if he would not go on without his brethren, so that "Moses called to them; and they turned to him". Paul speaks of the shining of the face of Jesus, there was nothing to repel. Paul himself was exactly like that to the Corinthians, a bondman "for Jesus sake".
W.C. "I drew them with bands of a man, with cords of love" (@Hosea 11: 4). Is that the thought of the covenant?
J.T. Quite so. The allusion is to how God served Israel through Moses and Aaron.
J.J. Is the climax reached in @Psalm 148: 14 "the children of Israel, a people near unto him", and then @Psalm 149?
J.T. Quite so; it seems to begin in @Exodus 19, a people near to Jehovah. This matter of the letter of commendation opens up much about the new covenant, both in regard to the saints and in regard to the minister. He leads them through other great things -- reconciliation and new creation -- so that now he says to them, "Our mouth is opened to you, Corinthians, our heart is expanded" (@2 Corinthians 6: 11).
Ques. In verse 2 of chapter 3, the apostle says, "*Ye* are our letter, written in our hearts, known and
{500}
read of all men". Does he suggest that the servant's commendation lies in his ability to view the saints, in the way you are speaking?
J.T. That is what is meant. The way Paul spoke about the Corinthians was wonderful; he would speak well of them, of how he regarded the work at Corinth. What is written in his heart is a wonderful thing; but now they also become an epistle "known and read of all men, being manifested to be Christ's epistle ministered by us".
The passage in @Deuteronomy 33 confirms what we have in these two scriptures, and corresponds with the book of Revelation. It seems to me that the Lord would keep the book of Revelation before us, as to the greatness of the saints and how they will appear. Paul says, we shall be "manifested with him in glory" (@Colossians 3: 4). John says, "the holy city, Jerusalem, coming down out of the heaven from God, having the glory of God" (@Revelation 21: 10). This brings out how great is the thought of what God has in us, in view of administration, in view of our millennial service. So that the ministers are mentioned, their names are on the foundations of the city. These men did their work well; it was foundational -- there were twelve of them -- and the result was seen. They did not go beyond their work, as it says, "To every man his work". In giving the twelve their work the Lord did not give them Paul's; but they had their own, which was not beyond the foundation. This wonderful superstructure reflects back on the apostles at Pentecost -- how well they did their work, and what men they were! They did not bring in the *Son of God*, but kept to their own line; not that they did not have the truth. A great principle in ministry is "to every man his work". So in @1 Corinthians 3 every man is to see how he builds, what he puts into it; the foundation is there.
{501}
A.J.G. Paul says, "For other foundation can no man lay besides that which is laid". Is the foundation which Paul laid consistent with the foundational work of the twelve?
J.T. It must be so, for whether in their work or his it is Christ. His name does not appear in the heavenly city. His work is not foundational in the primary general sense. He himself speaks in @Ephesians 2 of the foundation of the apostles and prophets, but his *name* is not there. He had part in the placing of the corner-stone, and, as he says in @1 Corinthians 3, he laid the foundation in a *practical* sense for his building among the gentiles. His work, therefore, would be included in @Ephesians 2: 20. The great thought that should impress us in considering these great servants, including Moses and David, is how feeling they were, how thoroughly they were with God, and had God's mind about those they were serving. So God says to Moses, "this people", but Moses says to God, "thy people". David, after the light of sonship had come to him, speaks of Israel in a wonderful way in the passage read in @2 Samuel 7: 23, 24: "Who is like thy people?"
J.H.B. David refers to the greatness of God Himself, saying, "Wherefore thou are great, Jehovah Elohim" (verse 22).
J.T. The whole passage, from verse 18 to the end, is a remarkable expression, by one who, as in the light of sonship, is full of the divine greatness; and correspondingly the greatness of God's people, for the greatness of the people is linked with the greatness of God.
