A.J.G. Is Paul in the spirit of that when he says, "*Ye* are Christ's"?
J.T. That brings out the true position. All things belong to the saints, they belong to Christ, and Christ belongs to God. That is the order seen in Corinthians.
Ques. Does the apostle observe the same principle
{502}
in his service manward? At Athens he says, "Being therefore the offspring of God ..."
J.T. It is *all* men there; he links man up with God. It seems to me that the greatness of God, in the economy into which He has come, requires the greatness of the saints, because it is a question of the whole scheme into which He has come, and the greatness of the saints is included in the shining out of God in His righteousness, power, love, and counsels.
Ques. Does David in @2 Samuel 7: 24 give the thought of God's inheritance in the saints? He says, "thou hast established to thyself thy people Israel to be a people unto thee for ever; and thou, Jehovah, art become their God".
J.T. That passage enters beautifully into what is before us.
J.T.S. Was it not the concern of these great servants that they should serve well? Paul says, "what is our hope, or joy, or crown of boasting? are not *ye* also before our Lord Jesus at his coming? for ye are our glory and joy" (@1 Thessalonians 2: 19, 20).
J.T. That is what we have been seeing. The twelve apostles names were in the foundation, and every man's name appears in relation to his work. We do not want to be ashamed of our work in that day.
{503}
THE SERVICE OF WOMEN IN LUKE'S GOSPEL
@Luke 8: 1 - 3; @Luke 2: 36 - 38; @Luke 1: 46 - 55
J.T. Other scriptures in Luke entering into our subject are more familiar to us perhaps than these, and we shall need to touch on them. The first is in chapter 4; Peter's wife's mother who "served them" after she was healed. Then the woman in chapter 7, well known to us, who loved much and served accordingly. Then Martha, in chapter 10, who also served, but was herself in a complaining critical state; and then the women at the sepulchre, mentioned in chapter 24, who would serve the Lord, but as thinking He was dead when He was already alive. They were serving ignorantly, and hence to no purpose.
We should see to begin with what is mentioned in chapter 8, namely, that service in women is seen as related to *the new system of things* which the Lord had inaugurated in principle, so that we may see that, in whatever way we serve in detail, we are serving to one purpose, serving in relation to the administrative system that has been set up. We are not to be independent or isolated in our thoughts, but having one end in view; all is to be done in relation to the Lord *and* those representing His authority -- the twelve. The passage says that the twelve were with Him; alluding to this official system into which His authority entered; authority which must extend to all service. So it says that the twelve were with Him, and the passage shows that the "certain women" were related to them in principle -- "certain women who had been healed of wicked spirits and infirmities, Mary who was called Magdalene, from whom seven demons had gone out, and Joanna, wife of Chuza, Herod's steward, and Susanna
{504}
and many others, who ministered to him of their substance" (@Luke 8: 2, 3). They were with Him; not seen here as serving in their houses or in any isolated way, but in relation to Him and the twelve.
Then another thing to be noticed is what Zacharias says, speaking by the Spirit, in chapter 1: "... that, saved out of the hand of our enemies, we should serve him without fear ..." These remarks by Zacharias, I think, govern what we are speaking of in regard to the state in which we serve, that is, "... to give us, that, saved out of the hand of our enemies, we should serve him without fear in piety and righteousness before him all our days" (verses 74, 75). The object in view, therefore, is to see how Luke links up the service seen in the women in his gospel with the system of things Christ has inaugurated. Of course it refers to men, too, but the thought is to bring out the subjective conditions which women usually represent. This system seems to fit in after the wonderful appreciation of grace shown in chapter 7; the great grace chapter, as we might say -- not only of grace shown, but grace *valued*, grace *received*. She is forgiven much, hence serves much; loves much, and serves accordingly, as if that sort of result of the gospel must immediately be connected with the systematised order of things which God has set up, for it must not be linked with man's organisations, it must be linked with God's. It is too great for anything else, and every true believer in the gospel is in this position. It speaks of Mary, from whom seven demons had gone out. It would seem that it is not the act of power that is stressed here, but rather the condition that had arisen. So that the position of the demons was untenable -- they had *gone* out.
C.G. Does the sense of grace in the heart, which finds its expression in the woman and what she did
{505}
in the previous chapter, enter into our assembly service?
J.T. That is what I thought; that chapter 8 is spiritually needed; the system is needed for such a result of the gospel. The Lord turns to the woman in speaking to Simon, as if to rebuke, on the one hand, the wretched attitude of the natural religious man, and on the other to extol the result of grace in the believer. He turns to the woman, as if *she* is the person of importance now. The legal system which Simon represented is left; is turned away from; the woman is now the material; the kind of personnel that belongs to the new system, and it must be provided; nothing less will do.
M.W.B. Does the fact that the divine system is necessarily connected with the service imply that, though begun individually, in its normal development, the service of God must be collective?
J.T. That is exactly what I had in mind as seen in the beginning of chapter 8. Notice it is "the glad tidings of the kingdom of God", that is the appellation by which the gospel is described here, involving system, too -- the kingdom of God. Then the twelve were with Him, and certain women who ministered to Him *of their substance*. Of course it would be material things, but substance, from the point of view of which we are speaking, would be spiritual.
Rem. It says, they were healed of wicked spirits, and of infirmities, as though these two conditions had been met, the power of evil on the one hand, and also the matter of infirmities.
J.T. Well, that is salvation in a double way, from the power of evil and from illnesses or infirmities. The latter would hinder, too; we may be delivered from the demons and yet not from the infirmities.
Rem. It would appear that those who served in this way, as you are suggesting as to the women,
{506}
follow the Lord's movements in detail from city to city and village to village.
J.T. Yes, it shows, I suppose, the energy in the service. Later on we are told that the women by the cross had followed Him from Galilee -- it shows the energy of affection.
W.H.U. Is the securing of these women for service in any way connected with the activities of the anointed Man?
J.T. You mean beginning with chapter 4, where He is seen as anointed. Yes, quite so. That brings us to the first case we have noted, namely, the mother-in-law of Simon. That was household service, as you might say. She was sick of a fever, and she is one of those who are healed. Demon power is not noted there, but the bodily disease is -- the fever. It was evidently a case of insubjection, which, of course, prevents effective service. So it is said, the Lord stood over her -- "Standing over her, he rebuked the fever, and it left her; and forthwith standing up she served them". There were immediate results, showing how the beneficiaries of grace immediately join in in spreading the thought -- "She served them".
A.F.M. Would the standing over her imply *authority*, particularly over the origin of the disease or weakness?
J.T. I think it does. Of course authority might be expressed in a mere official, but here it is a question of the Person who exercised it; the moral power or authority in the Lord Jesus standing over her -- such an One as He.
Eu.R. Is that suggested in the way Luke presents Him at the outset, "thou shalt call his name Jesus ... and the Lord God shall give him the throne of David his father"? -- it is His moral worth and beauty that give Him ascendency over us.
J.T. I think so; in this particular case it is in
{507}
the house, and He is there on friendly terms of course, in Simon's house. Arbitrary authority may be seen in any one, even an unconverted man, but there is no moral authority in it at all; that is, the person does not convey the thing, but here the Person is in mind, such a person as is described, and anointed in the previous chapter. They took notice of Him, too; His demeanour in the synagogue and the kind of words that were coming out of His mouth; it is the Man Himself that is in mind; so that the woman is evidently impressed spiritually. We can understand that feverish conditions need this moral power in those who are seeking to serve. Immediate results follow -- she served *them;* it is a general thought -- she served all that were there.
A.P.T. Is this the public side of the truth?
J.T. I think so; it is what might be in any household. There are many causes of irritation in a household, which the fever would suggest, and what is needed is to bring moral weight, in the husband or in any one -- particularly the husband, into the house, that would subdue it and make it felt to be wrong in the insubject person.
J.P.H. In chapter 7 there is a man who said, "I also am a man placed under authority". Had he taken character from Christ, observing Him as a Man here subject to the blessed God, and he has his household in accord with that? Does that enter into the thought in chapter 8 that you have brought before us?
J.T. I think that is right. It is a remarkable illustration of light in a man's soul; he discerned, not only that the Lord had authority, but that it was delegated authority, tracing it back to God, even as his own authority would be traced back to the emperor. It shows how great a thought authority is, but authority expressed in a *word* -- not necessarily within the hearing of the person to be affected;
{508}
for the Lord did not go to the house -- it was a question of His word rather than His personal presence. This is an important side of the gospel that the word of Christ may affect one in any part of the world.
M.W.B. Is obedience the first feature, resulting from the recognition of authority? I was thinking of your reference to @Exodus 24, where, as you were saying, the thought of obedience, as seen in the twelve pillars, is essential.
J.T. Quite so; the Lord, I suppose, as standing over the woman, would represent the thought. He is here as the sent One; He could say, "The Spirit of the Lord is upon me, because he has anointed me to preach glad tidings to the poor; he has sent me ..." He is here in subjection Himself and conveying it palpably as standing over the woman. Hence the husband is not exercising his authority arbitrarily, but with Christ as his Head; all is traced back to God; God is Christ's Head.
F.W.W. Would this woman, Peter's wife's mother, be illustrative of one who has accepted the glad tidings of the kingdom of God?
J.T. Yes. So in @1 Corinthians 11 the word "authority" is used; the woman is to have "authority on her head". This would evidently allude to the thing itself -- the symbol of the authority being in another. She is fully recognising the authority that comes down from God through Christ to the man.
Rem. It says in Mark, "they came with James and John into the house of Simon and Andrew. And the mother-in-law of Simon lay in a fever".
J.T. It is well to bring in Mark's side, for, in inviting people, we ought to have consideration for the department of the wife, what she is capable of.
J.J.J. What would be the difference between obedience and subjection, or is there no difference?
{509}
J.T. Subjection is an attitude of soul as to a person; of course obedience is, too, but obedience is a readiness to obey any word that comes from Him. Subjection is there whether there is a command or not; it is attitude of mind.
Rem. The Lord had already exercised His authority in the synagogue in relation to the unclean spirit, but it is still the same authority, and from the same source.
J.T. The same authority, but as seen in the house. Conditions were evidently better in Simon's house than in the synagogue. It is the same day.
A.F.M. Do you think the movements of the Lord in chapter 8 flow from the end of the seventh, showing the spiritual energy that marked Him in the evangelical work He took up?
J.T. Yes, we were remarking on that at the beginning that what the end of the seventh presents requires the eighth; it requires a new system for such love as this. He turns to the woman -- she is now an object for His mind and heart; He turns to her, He is occupied with that kind of person; He turns away from Simon. She is of the kind that are mentioned in this chapter: "certain women who had been healed of wicked spirits and infirmities, Mary who was called Magdalene, from whom seven demons had gone out" -- she belongs to that class.
Ques. Does she link with the woman in chapter 21 who cast her all into the treasury? Her affections being secured, in chapter 7, she becomes contributory to the divine system.
J.T. Quite so; I think it is a similar thought, that the temple must be destroyed, as indicated in chapter 21, in view of what would suit that woman -- such giving as hers.
A.P.T. Is the first verse of chapter 8 linked on with the book of Numbers as supported by this love position set up in Exodus?
{510}
J.T. That is good. The system is seen in the first part of Numbers set up, but it is also seen, of course, in the tabernacle at the end of Exodus and Jehovah speaking out of it in the beginning of Leviticus. In Numbers it is the fellowship, all set up in relation to the sanctuary; God in it; God among you of a truth, and service is in that relation, whether we are priests or military men.
Eu.R. Has this idea of ministering to the Lord a priestly touch about it? Could we link it up with @Acts 13: 1 - 3, where they were ministering to the Lord and fasting, and the Holy Spirit said, "Separate me now Barnabas and Saul"? Have you there the system established and inaugurated in the Lord Jesus personally?
J.T. Yes, it seems to correspond.
Eu.R. So, when you speak of a system at the present time, it would take the form of assemblies in localities all the world over.
J.T. That is the next thing. It brings us to chapter 10, for taking possession of the land, I think, involves assemblies, that is, the authority and order of God set up in localities -- it is only thus that the position can be held. The land is taken, as it were, but Paul and Barnabas make a point of assemblies and choosing elders in each of them, so as to have a state of things that could be dependable. The idea in selecting territory is, I think, that divine rights are set up, what God is going to bring in in a public way presently, is now here in testimony. In this well-known passage in the end of @Luke 10, that is, in the house of Martha whose sister was Mary of Bethany. It is evidently the locality that is in mind, because it says, "it came to pass as they went that *he* entered into a certain village ..." The way this matter is treated, both here and in John, indicates plainly that it is a locality that is in mind. Things are not right; Martha is not right,
{511}
but she recognises the Lord. It is as if a servant might come into a town, and the brethren recognise him, receive him, perhaps, into some house, but then the state of the meeting would soon appear in the way he is treated and what is extended to him. So here the Lord is admitted into the house, and indeed Martha calls Him, "Lord" -- she respects Him in that way, and yet she is critical of Him. Obviously His position in the place would be very uncomfortable. There is one there, on the other hand, who is thoroughly appreciative of Him, but she is not serving; Martha is the serving element, and it is not right -- that is the lesson here, I think. There is the general administrative position, and all service is to be carried on in that relation, but then the question is, What are the local conditions in each place -- Colosse, Philippi, Corinth, Ephesus, etc.; that is what is to be learnt in chapter 10, and how the Lord takes the matter on. John has a more abstract way of presenting things, and he shows that conditions in Bethany became perfect as regards service. God is not content with anything less than a perfect state of things -- perfect in principle, anyway, though not perhaps in detail. John has a way of presenting perfection, ignoring anything incongruous.
Ques. Lydia was one who worshipped God; would her house at Philippi answer to what you are suggesting? Having gone out of the prison Paul came to her -- would that suggest what you are saying as to being perfect?
J.T. I think so. That is very good. According to @Luke 10, the ministers, the seventy, would go to a place, in view of the Lord coming after them, and in coming to a town they would seek out a son of peace -- that is the principle of ministry. If a son of peace be there you are to stay with him, and I think Lydia was a son of peace in Philippi. The
{512}
apostle must have been very uneasy at Philippi, although he found the woman, but Lydia was what was sought in a positive way, as a son of peace; they were delighted to stay in that house. It is to show how general conditions flow out of household conditions amongst the brethren. So the next man, the jailor, is spoken to about the house; he and his house were to be saved; so the salvation of the house, as well as the brother, is the point there. He spoke to the jailor's house the word of the Lord, not simply the commandment, but the *word*, that which conveys authority in the house.
Ques. Would the gain of the kingdom bring in those conditions you speak of?
J.T. Yes. There are two things mentioned about it, it is a very thorough matter -- city by city and village by village; then, preaching and announcing, or showing. The two words used as to the service show that the truth is thoroughly presented.
W.W. What would you say as to the transfiguration in that connection? Does the Lord bring that in to set out the sphere of things in which we are to serve?
J.T. Quite so, to see what was presented -- the kingdom of God -- how influential it was. Mary is apparently moving in that light, sitting at His feet, hearing His word; that was what was said on the mount in Luke, "Hear him".
Eu.R. What you are saying seems to imply that the underlying state as seen in Mary may be much more healthy than the serving element, but the Lord would patiently adjust the serving element so that the service might proceed unhinderedly.
J.T. I think that is the thing. Luke makes much of "city by city, and village by village". So in chapter 10 He would follow upon the service of the seventy.
A.P.T. The Lord says, "Martha, Martha". Does
{513}
that show His intense feeling, and is that seen also in His standing over the woman in Simon's house?
J.T. Yes. Martha is a difficult case, but you see the finished product in @John 12. This speaks very loudly to those who serve; it is really the whole subjective condition of things in the place that is in mind, the women suggesting that.
M.W.B. In @Joshua 8: 34, 35, to which you have referred, it says, "afterwards he read all the words of the law, the blessing and the curse, according to all that is written in the book of the law. There was not a word of all that Moses had commanded which Joshua read not before the whole congregation of Israel, and the women, and the children, and the strangers that lived among them". Is there a link there with local conditions today?
J.T. Yes; that is the ground on which the land is taken possession of. God will give us possession of it on His own terms, as laid down in Deuteronomy. They are recognised in Joshua 8.
M.W.B. The thought is carried out today in local assemblies.
J.T. That is how the matter stands. So they chose them elders -- the saints did not choose the elders, they never do, the apostles chose them elders; trustworthy men who would rule well and hold things for God.
When we come to @Luke 24, the women observed the sabbath and remained quiet. They were lovers of Christ, but they were serving ignorantly; their spices were not needed for a living Christ, they were thinking of a dead Christ; the Lord did not need any such attention. He was living. There are many like that, who are serving ignorantly to no end at all.
M.W.B. Therefore the question, "Why seek ye the living one among the dead?"
J.T. Quite so. Well now, having spoken of all
{514}
these cases, perhaps the ground is clear to consider the more spiritual side in Luke, which he begins with indeed. He begins with spiritual service.
W.S. Would Mary bring out the spiritual side of service in perfection?
J.T. That is right. Anna is the first one to mention -- leaving Mary for the final thought. Anna is presented as a model in the sense of continuance in the thing; Mary comes into it quickly or immediately, but Anna is presented to us as a model of continuance of energy to old age. The service is not taken up late in life with Anna, the formation of the vessel is in mind; the whole history of the woman, a long history, but entirely commendable from the outset -- a beautiful character.
Ques. Would the seven years and the eighty-four years show increase?
J.T. I suppose the seven years with a husband would mean that she had adorned the wifely position; she had adorned the earlier position, too -- the young woman's position. The eighty-four years, I apprehend, refers to the whole period of her widowhood, and would mean that she adorned those circumstances. It is an honourable mention as to the person in the three outstanding periods of her life: a virgin's position, a wife's position, and a widow's position. The woman is tested in that way, her personality is tested; God has watched over it, and now shows that it culminated in the most beautiful, and energetic, and intelligent service, night and day in the temple; she "did not depart from the temple, serving night and day with fastings and prayers". What a history that was, night and day serving in the temple!
A.P.T. It is remarkable that the night comes first.
J.T. Yes, that would be the more testing side.
Ques. Is it the kind of adornment Peter speaks
{515}
of in regard to women, which is in the sight of God of great price (@1 Peter 3: 4)?
J.T. Yes.
Ques. Would this woman Anna be one who would be known? You have spoken of those who are known in heaven, known by God.
J.T. Quite so. "if any man love God, the same is known of him" (@1 Corinthians 8: 3). So she had a great place in heaven, was well known there. That is a side of the truth that perhaps is little thought of, the history of things in heaven and how much part we have in it. We are left out of angelic history, but we are to understand something of human history in heaven, the history that men make in heaven. So when Jesus went into heaven, it was a wonderful time, and so all that has gone on ever since. Now we have the manhood of Christ in heaven, the kind of Man God has in His counsels. What movement there must have been as Christ went into heaven! The Lord said, "your names are written in heaven", and surely this woman would have a great place there.
A.P. She would be a stranger in the world system, but known to all that looked for redemption in Israel.
W.H.U. Does she, in any way, represent the service of the sanctuary?
J.T. I think she does. She has no other object now, the temple was everything to her because God was there. Then there are such sacrifices, such self-renunciation, in fasting and prayers night and day in the temple, and she is honoured now in that she does not miss the greatest occasion ever seen in the temple.
W.H.U. Is it necessary to get beyond the sacrificial side, to get to the full thought in the service of God? We are very much occupied with the death
{516}
of Christ as to what He has done for us, and perhaps miss the blessedness of what He *is*.
J.T. "She spoke of him". She would have gathered up all the wonderful speakings of the Old Testament, because from the very outset there is a speaking about Jesus all the way down. I suppose she may be taken to represent what is cumulative, as Stephen was; he gathered up things, and she spoke of Christ according to the intelligence the Spirit would give of the Old Testament teaching.
R.C. Do fasting and prayer need to precede this?
J.T. I think so, fasting shuts yourself out and prayer brings God in; the fasting makes room for the prayer that brings God in.
Ques. Would her speaking of him to all those who waited for redemption in Jerusalem, suggest service in an assembly setting?
J.T. I think that is right, for this idea of speaking to all that looked for redemption in Jerusalem would suggest a sphere of interest. She was not alone, nor are we ever alone on these lines. There was a right state of soul in those she spoke to or they would not have been looking for redemption. God is working in people who are looking for what He promises.
Ques. Does she stand in contrast to the two going to Emmaus, where the Lord had to interpret to them the things concerning Himself?
J.T. Just so. She evidently would know something of the value of the sacrifices.
Rem. Simeon is ready to go, but Anna would continue.
J.T. She suggests what Paul says, "our whole twelve tribes". It is what faith grasps -- "our whole twelve tribes" serving God.
Eu.R. Would "our whole twelve tribes" involve @1 Corinthians 10: 17, "we being many are one bread, and one body;" faith would always claim every
{517}
saint on earth for that, and make a way to secure it in the assemblies?
J.T. Quite so. Colossians says, Love for all saints. Anna shows, I think, how it would continue. It is seen in this longevity given in three sections.
B. Would Hannah in 1 Samuel be on a parallel line? We have her prayer making room for God.
J.T. Yes. It is called a prayer, but there is not a word of what we speak of as prayer in it. It is a rich contribution to the service of God, really. The whole book issues from Hannah's exercises leading up to the service of God through David.
I thought we might finish with Mary in Luke 1. It is what comes in immediately; it is not a long history in the service of God, it is a young person, but she can say, "The Mighty One has done to me great things" -- it is the outcome of what is immediate -- what the Mighty One has done; what God may effect quickly in anyone; for He does at times bring about quick results, and I think she is a remarkable representation of this. There is a suggestion here of a holy fellowship; she goes to her cousin Elizabeth in the hill country -- an elevated position. It is the elevated heavenly side where holy intercourse between brethren is known -- sisters in this case. Mary's voice fell on the ear of Elizabeth conveying what she was personally -- "the mother of my Lord". She was a subject person; she said to the angel, "be it to me according to thy word", and now we have the rich fruit of the work of God in her. I think we see it in the relations between her and Elizabeth, and at the grave of Christ. It is a touch we can understand and shows the wonderful link, as a result of which the babe leaps with joy at the salutation of this person. What a person God has made this woman! So it is in measure in any believer, what God may bring about immediately. What an immediate result was produced in Lydia
{518}
by the power of God! In others it may take longer to reach a finished product.
M.W.B. Would Mary illustrate, in the way you have been speaking of her, one who could present an offering, but with it, a meat offering and a drink offering, as seen in @Numbers 15?
J.T. Yes. It is not simply that the Spirit came upon her as in Elizabeth's case. We are told, "Elizabeth was filled with the Holy Spirit;" Elizabeth represents one on whom the Spirit might come at any time, as in a meeting, perhaps beyond a person's state. The Spirit came in connection with Mary's voice, but Mary is the immediate result of the power of God; showing how an immediate result may be reached in a person by the power of God. Elizabeth is touched by her voice, and room is made by her touch for the Spirit, as it were, but Mary is a finished product already, without the long experience of Anna. Even Elizabeth is a comparatively old person, but Anna is seen as representing a long and honourable experience, reaching a great occasion. Mary has not that, but she has what is finished, and we have the actual composition with which she serves. It is really equal to Hannah's prayer; it is very like it, but it is not presented as the immediate result of the Spirit coming upon her, it is more what God has made her, what a person she is by the mighty power of God affecting her.
Eu.R. Do you link the state God had brought about in Mary so quickly with the thought of the one body?
J.T. I would not like to build up much as to the one body; it is a question of an organism.
Eu.R. Do you mean if there is this state with us in the assembly we shall affect each other spiritually as Mary affected Elizabeth?
J.T. Just so. You are speaking, and you are conscious of something being revealed to one sitting by,
{519}
and you sit down; that would show sensitiveness as to the power of transmission. I believe the organism underlies all that follows chapter 10 of 1 Corinthians. The power of transmission is so tender and delicate that the body is likened to that. It is not an organisation, but an organism with the most delicate means of transmission -- they are inscrutable really.
Rem. There had not been experience with Mary, but a very careful divine selection, and therefore the Lord has a person He can do a very rapid work with. It was the same with regard to Timothy; we get his genealogy mentioned, and the apostle can say he has no man like-minded.
J.T. He comes to fruition quickly. What is presented here, I believe, is the work of God in her, what the Mighty One had done, a result immediately produced. What Mary said, in @Luke 1: 46 - 55 is a production, a contribution to the service of God; her own composition. It is the immediate effect of the Mighty One's operations -- that He had a vessel that could produce such a contribution. We get it here as an example for us as in God's service.
Rem. She links Israel with it and goes right back to Abraham, embracing all God's thoughts.
J.T. Yes, so far as they are indicated. She is able to carry down the thoughts of God. See what a place they have in her soul, how she can put them out in such beautiful words!
A.P.T. Would it be in your line of thought to introduce the summit reached in @1 Corinthians 14 -- the whole assembly gathered together in one place?
J.T. Quite so. One has something -- "each of you has a psalm", etc.; but then there is one sitting by able to receive a communication by the Spirit. Simeon holds the Child in his arms, and had an
{520}
immediate word regarding Him. It is better to receive a communication as coming into the temple by the Spirit, than to bring something of your own; not that this is out of place, but, as coming by the Spirit, I am ready for anything the Spirit may give me.
Rem. You have in mind, in Mary, what is possible in a subject person.
J.T. Yes, that is the thought. She says, "Behold the bondmaid of the Lord; be it to me according to thy word". Then the angel departed from her, suggesting that she could be relied upon.
Rem. The finished product would be a vessel of praise for God?
J.T. Quite so.
W.B. Mary had benefited from what had gone before, and in that way was a suitable vessel for the service of God.
C.G. In Hannah in the Old Testament and in Anna and Mary here do we get levitical exercises? Hannah says, "For this boy I prayed", Anna "spoke of him;" and of Mary we are told that she kept the things relative to the Lord, and pondered them in her heart.
J.T. That is an important part of the subject. The point in Mary is the effect of the Mighty One's operations. She became subsequently a vessel, what is called a "treasury" in the types -- that is, she kept things in her mind and pondered them in her heart. So that she is seen in the inauguration of the assembly in the Acts, the only woman mentioned specifically; she had treasure that no one else could have; the precious knowledge that she, as His mother, had of Christ from the outset.
{521}
TEACHING
@John 8: 25 - 30; @John 14: 26; @John 16: 12 - 14; @Acts 2: 42
I have in mind to speak of *teaching;* to speak indeed of what has been rightly called the divine economy, into which many features of divine administration enter, but I wish to speak of teaching as one of the great features of the economy.
John enlarges on the divine administrative system more than the other evangelists, and in a peculiar way, making it evident that it is the outcome of love, that it is a matter between divine Persons, the Father being the source of all. John's ministry, particularly his gospel, has in mind the family side. The saints are being prepared, and indeed have been for many years, from the beginning of what we might rightly call the latter-day revival, for the family side and have been nourished in it. John goes to the root of things in his gospel in centring everything, or rather rooting everything, in the Father, not only the Father in power and wisdom, but in love. John tells us that "the Father loves the Son".
There can be no doubt that it was divinely anticipated that the idea of "father" would enter into the great hierarchical systems which began to take root from an early period in our dispensation; the word "father" has had great place in these systems. Indeed, the expression 'the fathers', regarded as authorities, has a great place in the vocabulary of the order of things I have alluded to, and the title father is taken on by a large proportion of its priests. This assumption of the title is over against our Lord's direct word: "Call no man your father upon the earth", and that commandment is being ignored. The idea of father has thus laid hold of, and given character to, this great development now extending back seventeen hundred years or more.
{522}
One can discern that the Spirit of God had in mind in John's gospel the overthrow of all this in the last days, indeed from the beginning, but particularly that the saints, as having been deflected for centuries, should be recovered to the feature of the divine economy conveyed in the expression "Father and Son", and to what develops from it -- Father and sons and Father and children.
That is what I have in mind, and particularly in relation, as I said, to teaching, to show how teaching emanates from the Father, that is what the Lord Himself says in the verse read in John 8. If there are those here who are in any way held by the development I have alluded to, one would refer for their sakes, and for the sake of all of us, to Jonathan; for in teaching, as in all other respects of the work entering into this dispensation, it is a question of the Father. The Lord says, "My Father worketh hitherto and I work" (@John 5: 17); and again, "The Son can do nothing of himself" -- a marvellous statement! -- and "whatever he sees the Father doing ... these things also the Son does in like manner". That is, all emanates from the Father, whether it be direct injunction and teaching or example.
Jonathan's was a dark day in Israel's history, a day made dark by his incorrigible father, Saul, even as our days are made dark by the incorrigibleness of the element I have alluded to; the whole position was darkened, and spiritually made desperate. Israel was beset on every hand by the Philistine armies issuing forth as they would, the soldiers of Saul even wanting in the ordinary implements of war and having to go to their enemies for what they could get. In the midst of such a state of things Jonathan represents those who work with God, and that, dear brethren, is the point: that we are workers with God whatever our service, whether preaching, or teaching, exercising eldership, or any
{523}
form of service, whatever it be, the prime thought surely is to be workers with God. Our work will not be effective otherwise, it will be worse than none, as the Lord says, "he that gathers not with me scatters". So Jonathan understood fighting in an individual sense, being a man of faith, and the sequel brought out that it was a movement of faith and that he was a worker with God, and, he *told not his father*. This means that the development I have spoken of is not to be consulted as regards any work of God, it is to be ignored -- Jonathan told not his father.
I need not say that he had to say to God; he worked with God, and if we are to work with God we must have to say to Him, to pray to our Father really in secret. We shall learn there who the real Father is. A word for anyone of us, especially young ones, is, "enter into thy chamber", as we are told by the Lord. The Lord Himself says, "and having shut thy door, pray to thy Father who is in secret, and thy Father who sees in secret will render it to thee" (@Matthew 6: 6). Here in John 8, as you will observe, His remarks to the Jews were not understood. "They knew not that he spoke to them of the Father". The Father, I need not say, was always nearest to His heart and uppermost in His mind, but they do not understand, as the Lord presses the point, that the economy is a question of the Father and the Son, and that all emanates from the Father, the Son's place in the economy being one of entire subjection and infinitely real, including even His being taught of the Father. In chapter 5 He tells us that "The Son can do nothing of himself save whatever he sees the Father doing". How marvellous that this should be said! And why is it said? Is it not to impress us with the reality of the Lord's position as the sent One, as taking the place of learning everything, and hearing everything,
{524}
receiving communications in regard to everything from His Father?
This in no way, I need not add, beclouds His holy, divine Personality; that is always intact, remaining in its inscrutability, so that here in chapter 5, He asserts that all must honour the Son as they honour the Father -- a remarkable combination of facts! On the one hand, He asserts that He could do nothing Himself, and on the other that all must honour the Son as they honour the Father. Let us not be afraid of these statements, dear brethren, but let us look at them in a holy, subject, intelligent way, for without the understanding of them we shall be weak as regards our position in the economy. We are all brought into it as believers, but we are brought into it according to what we are, each in his little measure -- the economy includes the Father, the Son, the Spirit, the apostles, and then others of us according to our measure. It is a question of graded authority -- delegated authority all emanating from the Father, and as real as delegated to Christ, and as real as delegated to the Spirit, to the apostles, or to any of us according to our measure.
So that the Lord says that He can do nothing of Himself save what He sees the Father doing, and that the Father shows Him all things that He Himself does, and that He would show Him greater works even than these which He was doing; that as the Father raised the dead and quickened them so should the Son. We can thus see that the Lord in asserting the immense stoop He had taken to carry out the will of the Father and to establish the economy, at the same time asserts His own equality with the Father. There can be no question about that, so that He says, "that all may honour the Son, even as they honour the Father". And yet there is the most perfect subjection, in everything that He
{525}
did, He saw the Father doing it, or heard from the Father about it.
Then as regards teaching, our passage says, verse 28, "When ye shall have lifted up the Son of man, then ye shall know that I am he, and that I do nothing of myself, but as the Father has taught me I speak these things". I am speaking now of teaching: "I do nothing of myself", the Lord says. You may say, I cannot take it in! Well, believe it any way; face it. The understanding of the economy necessitates that we take in these wonderful words in regard of Himself; the reality of the stoop He took; His obedience as the sent One, so that there should be no question in our minds as to how real the matter is. It implies delegated authority, as seen in the centurion's remarks in Luke 7. The Lord, he said, did not need to go to his servant, but only to speak the word, and he should be healed; "For I also am a man placed under authority, having under myself soldiers", he said. The centurion being in a position of authority as a Roman official, understood the idea of delegated authority, for the Roman system was externally very like the divine system in that way, and it is about to be developed again -- the trinity of evil in which everything shall be centred in a head, under a father. The cry was, Release unto us Barabbas -- the son of the father -- that is coming into evidence again in christendom; there is an imitation, and all will emanate from the idea of a father.
Here we have this marvellous statement of the Lord Jesus, "I do nothing of myself, but as the Father has taught me I speak these things". Think of the Lord Jesus saying this, bearing in mind what I have said, that there is no change in the Personality, He is equal with the Father. It is said in chapter 5 that they persecuted Him because He was "making himself equal with God" -- but *He is*! He
{526}
is God Himself, but, in the position He has taken in the economy, He is in absolute dependence; there is no question about it, and as we truly accept it, as a doctrine, and as a principle, the more we shall understand the economy, and the more we shall fit into it, each according to his measure -- for every believer ought to fit -- but we cannot fit in save on the principle of absolute subjection. It comes down from the Father to the Son, through the Spirit, and through the apostles, and then according to each one's measure, to everyone, to the feeblest christian amongst us.
I am speaking now of teaching and how the Lord took that place, and in taking it He established what I am speaking of, a state and attitude of absolute subjection and dependence. That all comes down from the Father, and so He goes on to say, "he that has sent me is with me; he has not left me alone, because I do always the things that are pleasing to him". That is all an example for us, showing that in subjection and dependence, we shall not be without divine support, nor companionship either; the Lord will ever be found alongside of us; we shall never be alone in our service, as learning from Him, and then what flows out from that is that we do those things that please the Father. Each one is making history in heaven as he serves on these lines; the Father has pleasure in every one of us as we do.
This other thing that I have been speaking of, this development, that has now become so rampant, is another authority, but it is to be ignored. Jonathan did not tell his father, nor should any one of us as in this wonderful system of things which is embedded in love -- permeated by love. Why should I tell the father? Why should I recognise such arrogated authority as if I were obligated to it? I am not to do it, but I am to proceed in my service
{527}
according to the pattern set out in John's gospel, and I am making history in heaven every moment as I do so, and the Father is pleased with me. Can anything be more delightful than the sense that the Father is pleased with me? He was infinitely so with Jesus, not only as His equal, not only as the One He loved before the world was, but as the One who now had taken this lowly place, carrying out the service of teaching as Himself being taught by the Father. Let us not be afraid of these words, I am quoting the Lord's own words. It is necessary that we should take them into our souls, and understand the position He took, and what an example He set for us. God intends that we are to come into this, and to afford Him pleasure, each in his own measure as moving in the sense of being representative of God and having delegated authority. You say, What! delegated authority to me, with no gift! Well, beloved, we have the idea of giving a cup of cold water "in the name of a disciple" -- a disciple is something, he is representative -- representative of teaching. The saints acquire their proper place in our minds by what they are in this way, as having graded authority.
We are vested with power. The Lord gave the authority to his bondmen, also "unto every one his work;" so that the work goes on in a way pleasing to heaven. It is not simply that something is accomplished, the first thing is to be pleasing to God, pleasing to the Father. So that the Lord says, "he that has sent me is with me; he has not left me alone, because I do always the things that are pleasing to him".
Well now, one may say, Very little would be done if we took these lines, if we ignored human methods that are so successful in evangelical work and missionary enterprise. We may truly get little outward result in these days, but the first great result at all
{528}
times is to please God. That is the point the Lord makes; He says, "I do always the things that are pleasing to him", and "As he spoke these things many believed on him". That surely is a result, it is not a question of gift, He is not preaching, or teaching, He is merely stating facts as to the economy, and evidently faith was operative because it says, "As he spoke these things many believed on him".
I now go on to the Spirit in chapter 14: 26: "the Comforter, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in my name, *he* shall teach you all things, and will bring to your remembrance all the things which I have said to you". The Lord, as I have said, takes the ground of being taught of the Father, and then He teaches the apostles, which I will refer to in a moment. All the gospels are full of the Lord's teaching, and in this gospel His service in this respect has a peculiar charm. In Matthew it is more authoritative, chapter 5: 1, 2, involving also more exercise on the part of the disciples, a side of the truth which is important, although we learn also in restfulness. There is that sort of learning, indeed; perhaps more of it is needed; that is, to lie fallow, absorbing things that the Lord teaches us, taking them in with our faculties all active. There can be no doubt that those who serve are hampered at times because of physical inability. The idea of a vessel that God uses is one in which the faculties are all available, unhampered by physical weakness; and hence those who serve do well to take it into account and not to fail to keep their sabbaths. One takes that home to oneself, it is an important matter to keep our sabbaths.
The sabbath idea is that one lies fallow and absorbs everything that may be available; as the soil absorbs the benefits of the environment, so the believer absorbs the wonderful benefits that flow
{529}
from the operations of divine Persons around him. There can be no doubt that that is needed, and it is perhaps needed more in those who are active in service than in saints generally. The saints generally need to be reminded of the need of exercise in order to grow spiritually. It is not simply that I read a book -- according to Matthew the Lord "went up into the mountain, and having sat down, his disciples came to him". They showed thus that they were interested, and as they came to Him, "having opened his mouth, he taught them". You see how communications from the Lord hinge on our interest -- thank God for so many here today and one assumes that all are interested! The Lord knows, and where there is interest, He never fails to open His mouth and teach, as He did in @Matthew 5 -- and He taught about *blessedness*, a very touching thing. Is there anyone in whom God is working who has no interest in being taught about blessedness? No commercial school, no university, no system of teaching of any kind can offer anything like this teaching in Matthew 5. The Lord opened His mouth and taught, and He taught about blessedness, as I said; He began to teach His disciples about the kind of people that are blessed. I want to be amongst these. There are about nine kinds of people He teaches about, that are all *blessed*.
It is one of the most important things in regard of our position, that we belong to a class of people who are blessed -- the Lord would teach us that. It is not simply that He would tell us, but He would *teach* us, so that the truth is forced into our souls, by gift or power; that is Matthew. We are told in Matthew, as we are indeed in Mark, that the Lord spoke with authority, and not as the scribes. Authoritative teaching is needed at a certain stage of our spiritual progress and particularly in view of the assembly. Matthew is the assembly gospel and
{530}
authoritative teaching is most essential to our place in the assembly, but John's side is more the home, the family thought: that I am taught where there is affection. Going up to the mountain does not suggest the place of affection, or the home; it suggests authority and it requires exercise. I see that I really need the teaching and go in for it, and in so doing I am taught that I am blessed. John would show us that Christ's teaching is very attractive, and so he tells us about two persons who followed Jesus -- the first two followers that Jesus had, and they called Him "Rabbi". The Spirit of God stops to tell us what the word means, so that there should be no doubt as to what these two followers of Jesus had in their minds. Then they said, "Where abidest thou?" and the Lord answered, "Come and see".
It is thus not a question of going up a mountain, but of coming to where He abides, and abiding with Him; that is the principle. That is an initial idea in John, to prepare us; and then follows what I have been speaking of, namely, the Father teaching the Son, and the Son teaching us directly and then through the Spirit. So that in John the Lord says to His enemies as they arrest Him, "I taught always in the synagogue and in the temple ... and in secret I have spoken nothing". He said, in effect, further, If that is so, why do you ask Me to convict myself? "Demand of those who have heard, what I have spoken" (@John 18: 21).
That is the ground to take, it is a question of the teaching. He taught, and who taught like Him! He said Himself, "They shall be all taught of God". He Himself took that place, and He teaches us, as carrying on the great thought of the administration, the "all things" which the Father has given to be in His hand. The educational department, so to speak, is looked after; He teaches, and the Spirit teaches as subordinate to Christ, for the Father sends
{531}
"another Comforter", as asked for by Christ, and also He sends Him in Christ's name. Notice it is the "Comforter", a word used as to the Spirit only in this gospel; "the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in my name, *he* shall teach you all things". So that we have the Spirit, a divine Person, here under Christ, for the Lord Himself also sends Him.
You will understand that each of the divine Persons may act as God. There are not three Gods, nor three Lords, there is one God and one Lord, but any one of the divine Persons may act as God. The Lord as the Son sends the Spirit, and the Spirit *comes* and teaches as to the Son, but the Father sends Him too, *He* sends the Comforter in the name of the Son, as I have said. Think of that precious thought, how the divine Persons are linked together in this wonderful matter, in carrying out what is being done for the divine will and pleasure at the present moment here below.
"The Comforter, the Holy Spirit, whom the Father will send in my name, *he* shall teach you all things". Why should I go to those fathers that I have been speaking about; you can understand why Jonathan did not tell his father; for it is a question of going on with God. The divine economy antedated all that I have spoken of. It is from the beginning; the Father, the Son, and the Spirit. Why should I go to any other father? the more I let them in, the worse for me. I am to proceed on my own line in relation to the Father, and the Son, and the Spirit, I am to hear what the Father says to me, then the Spirit is teaching me; He brings to our remembrance all that the Son said as down here, not only what is written. The Spirit knows everything, and He has everything, and He is opening up things to us that are not exactly written -- they are in keeping with what is written -- but the Spirit of God is a divine Person here, and brings all things
{532}
to our remembrance. He is the Comforter, and things are being opened up to us by the Comforter.
Now in @John 16, as you will notice, the Lord says further of Him, "when *he* is come, the Spirit of truth, he shall guide you into all the truth". *Guide you*! this word "guide" is to be noticed. Teaching may go on in a meeting like this; or in what we call a Bible reading, there may be ability to teach as the Spirit gives, and He loves to see those who have ability from Him, using their ability, one after another they may all speak -- it is delightful to heaven. You may say we had a good meeting, but heaven knows it better than we, for heaven has part in these meetings. The idea of the Comforter in this respect is that He is by us, and He is making the most of what everyone says. It is not teaching, it is guiding, so that one says one thing, and another says another, according to his measure, and the Spirit being present to guide us, the desired result is reached. We say, We had a good meeting, but what end did we reach and what truth was established? The truth is enforced if the Spirit has His way with us; something will be reached; something definite for the assembly. He guides and puts everything together and makes it one thing. This is what is meant when the Lord says, "But when *he* is come, the Spirit of truth, he shall guide you into all the truth: for he shall not speak from himself". Let us notice, that it is according to the economy; think of the grace of the Son and the Spirit taking such lowly attitudes. He will not speak of Himself, He speaks what He hears.
Let us take it in, even if we do not understand it. You say, I do not understand; well, accept it as the truth, and you will understand it afterwards. The Lord will give the understanding if we wait on Him. They are inspired words to teach us that the Spirit of God takes such a lowly attitude in this wonderful economy. The point for me is, am I taking such an
{533}
attitude myself, am I learning in my little niche to take it, acknowledging that all flows from the Father? I do not tell any other father, any other self-constituted authority; brethren have done it and have suffered for it, but it is not to be done, for the divine economy must stand, and if it does not stand, we shall not get anything. So the Spirit takes his place, and the Lord says, "he shall not speak from himself; but whatsoever he shall hear he shall speak; and he will announce to you what is coming". The word is "announce", but it is in the sense of making it plain, and how comforted we are as ministry makes the truth plain; it is evidently so, and carries its own weight with it, the Comforter is doing that, the Spirit of truth.
The Lord further says of Him, "He shall glorify me, for he shall receive of mine and shall announce it to you". See how blessedly the divine Persons are linked together -- Father and Son -- like Abraham and Isaac of old. The servant of the house tells Rebecca and her brother and her father's house, that all that Abraham has is Isaac's, and he called Isaac his master even as he called Abraham his master. John beautifully links the divine Persons together in this way, for he wants to bring us into this great economy of love, where we are pleasing to God in everything we are doing, as serving in love. "Faith ... works by love". The Spirit here, we are told, shall glorify Christ, and shows what He receives in the saints; and then the Lord says, "All things that the Father has are mine".
Well, finally I want to just show how this all worked out in the apostles, as seen in the verses we read in @Acts 2. The authority is now delegated to them; it is now in the hands of the apostles, who represent authoritativeness in teaching. The gospels give us the inwardness of all this, but this scripture gives us an account of how the apostles are serving,
{534}
and the Spirit of God gives us, in the verses which follow the one I read, a picture of the disciples -- those that believed Peter's preaching. Now what was it that marked them? Well, they persevered in the apostles teaching, and fellowship, and then in the breaking of bread and prayers. The first two ideas are apostolic. The apostles teaching and fellowship meaning that they have delegated authority -- the Lord gave His bondmen the authority -- and the converts understand that it is so. You say, How do they understand? Well, it is a feature of the work of God, that those who are the subjects of it, do understand; and so as Peter preached, those who were affected said to him, but they said to the other apostles as well, "men and brethren, what shall we do?" It is a feature of the work of God that persons are submissive to manifest authority.
The authority is not arrogated, it emanates from the Father, it has come down through the Son and the Spirit, and it is apparent in the twelve apostles. So it comes down to the present moment, and the authority is immediately apparent, in whomsoever it is found; it is accredited to the person who is ministering it. So the service is carried on as there is the authority in it which will make it effective. The converts at Jerusalem spoke to Peter, but the whole twelve apostles are recognised. How do they know that those other eleven had authority? How did they know they could teach? It was the work of God; one can tell in that way where God is working, and also where there is disregard of authority. If the manifest power of God is not working, then the flesh is active, but if God is really working there is always manifest subjection and recognition of what is here as representative of God. You say, Authority is in heaven, but it is down here, too; authority is delegated, and will be to the end. I have often quoted it, the Lord gave "to his bondmen the
{535}
authority", the authority is here still, and wherever God is working, there will be the recognition of it. However little there may be, let us understand this authority, and name it and bow to it.
These three thousand people that were affected by Peter's preaching asked for help, not only from him, but from the eleven; they recognised the authority of the eleven, they all had authority and power, and now it is not simply that they persevere in Peter's teaching, but in the teaching of the *apostles;* showing that Peter, James, John, Bartholomew, Matthew, Judas, and the others of the twelve, all had power; all these men worked; all these men were recognised by those in whom God was working, as having authority. What I am saying is verified in the fact that all their names are in the foundations of the heavenly city; and that is the testimony that each one of these men had authority and work given to him by Christ; the position that each has in the heavenly city is an unquestionable testimony that he did his work. We have no detailed account of the work of some of them, but what is in the heavenly city is unquestionable; there is the record, whatever silence there may be in history or in the Scriptures. The record is there indelibly, that these men did their work, and as the work of God began in Jerusalem, those who were of God recognised them.
So it is today, that however little power there is, any man, who is the subject of the work of God characteristically, will recognise it. Woe be to him that refuses it. The Lord is against such! Not that I am calling attention to anybody, I am speaking of the principles, of the wonderful dispensation that we are in -- the economy -- each bondman has his work, and the Lord gives power to us to carry out that work, and it is to be recognised. Well, that is all I have to say. The need for divine teaching is
{536}
great, and so here the testimony is that those converted were baptised, and were *added*, and they *persevered* in the teaching and fellowship of the apostles.
{537}
UPRIGHTNESS
@Galatians 2: 14; @2 Samuel 1: 17 - 19; @Joshua 10: 12 - 14; @Deuteronomy 33: 4, 5
My purpose is to speak about uprightness amongst the people of God. I have thus ventured to read the scriptures in 2 Samuel and in Joshua, relating to the book of Jasher, which word is said to denote uprightness, and, inasmuch as it is evidently a book of poetic productions to be conserved, the thought seems to be to teach us uprightness in an attractive manner, to make it attractive to us, to make it worth while. It leads to the collective thought in Jeshurun, which carries the thought not only of uprightness, according to its meaning given, but peculiar attractiveness, the word 'darling' being, as some think, suggested in its meaning. It is an upright people, not only an upright person, but an aggregation of such, and thus peculiarly attractive to Christ, He being also attractive to them. The more upright we are, the more we shall appreciate this feature in Jesus, who is the embodiment of it, as He is of every divine grace; hence He is in type King, we are told, amongst them -- "king in Jeshurun" -- but not simply amongst so many persons, but "When the heads of the people And the tribes of Israel were gathered together".
Well now, we have to look at this matter negatively, as indeed in such cases the negative side of it only enhances the positive quality involved. The quality is to be kept in mind, for, as I said, it culminates in the saints being viewed as lovely in Jeshurun, free from all those objectionable traits so developed, alas! in us, which sometimes cause us to be the opposite, causing certain ugliness, unattractiveness. Hence we are obliged to call attention to two great servants between whom the issue arose,
{538}
the issue of uprightness, or the absence of it, namely, Peter and Paul. I suppose we are entitled to regard them as the greatest servants of God without any exception: the one called the leading apostle among the twelve -- "first Peter;" the other our own apostle, to whom so much was committed, who said of himself by the Spirit that he "laboured more abundantly than they all".
The chapter from which I have read shows them together, first in council at Jerusalem in regard to a work of the enemy which had in view to introduce legality, involving indeed apostasy in which it reaches its full consummation; for the effort of the enemy was to add to the Galatian christians something that God did *not* add to them, but what He would subtract from them -- namely, legality. This epistle shows how even the great personality of Paul has to be thrown into the scale against this vicious doctrine which the apostle calls, "another gospel", and he said that any one who preached it should be accursed. There was no mincing of matters with him, so that he throws his whole personal weight into the scale against it, not only was there what he said against it -- most forceful words and epithets -- but his own personality is thrown into the scale against it; so that he says, in @Galatians 5: 2, "I, Paul, say to you, that if ye are circumcised, Christ shall profit you nothing". Think of that in view of modern public christianity! How the legalists, those who have returned to Judaism, and, alas! paganism, too, have cut themselves off, in principle at least, from "all profit from the Christ". In keeping with that remark, he says, "I, Paul" -- it is a question of his personality, what he was in the testimony, what he was personally, for he says, "by the grace of God I am what I am". He throws his weight, as I said, into the scale against this wicked doctrine
{539}
that had been propounded from Jerusalem and taught among the Gentiles.
It began early, by Satan employing what had been once accredited, to set aside the current thing that God had introduced, namely, christianity. It was a successful weapon of the devil to bring in *what had been* to set aside *what is*. We have to begin with *what is*, what is of God; hold to that whatever *has been*. We shall never understand what has been unless we understand what *is*. The apostle was contending for what was in his day, that is, christianity, as over against what had been, which was now polluted, adulterated, and used by the devil to set aside what then was, especially the ministry of the apostle Paul. We have to speak with feeling, in subdued tone, as to the great servant Peter being brought under the influence of this evil, if it were but for a little while. It surely is not written to discredit the great servant in our minds, it would be hidden for ever in oblivion were it not that the Lord intends that we shall gain by what happened; that the most spiritual of us should see how he is in danger of coming under an adverse influence, even if it be only for a while.
The apostle here, in the verses I read, brings in this thought of uprightness. He says, "when I saw that they do not walk straightforwardly, according to the truth of the glad tidings, I said to Peter before all ..." -- that is, he rebuked openly his great fellow servant. The chapter shows us what occurred at that memorable meeting at Jerusalem, when Paul and Barnabas taking Titus with them went up there and had a conference in regard to these matters. We get an insight as to how we are to stand with one another in the service of God, how we are to regard one another, and how careful we are to be as to those who are in distinction amongst us. The apostle says, he spoke "privately to those conspicuous
{540}
among them". We have to be on our guard as to what we say as to the Lord's servants; so the apostle spoke about those with distinction, but he says "to me those who were conspicuous communicated nothing". Yet there were Peter and James and John who seemed to be pillars -- they were outstanding even then. The apostle Paul, I am sure, had great satisfaction in discerning them at that time, and then he says, they discerned us too -- they recognised "the grace given to me". Happy indeed that brethren are so free from prejudice as to see what is of God in each other! for the gifts are varied and no gift can detract from another, in the abstract, anyway; it can only add to another. Increased gift rightly used is increased wealth, and we can only rejoice in every bit of ability the Lord gives. So Paul discerned that these three great servants were pillars. The word "pillar" carries its own meaning according to the use made of it in the testimony.
Then he says that "James, Cephas and John, [who were conspicuous as being pillars] recognising the grace given to me, gave to me and Barnabas the right hands of fellowship" -- a very happy situation, boding well for the work of God among the Gentiles; but, alas! subsequent influences diverted from this -- interfered with it. So far it was a happy state of things, and Peter came to Antioch evidently clear of these influences -- a great servant, as he was. As soon as Paul is on the scene, according to the historic account (Acts 9), Peter goes everywhere -- he went "through all quarters", meaning that he was instinctively in accord with this new great instrument that the Lord had introduced -- "an elect vessel", as He says of him, "to me", through whom He would establish the principle of local assemblies. Peter went through all quarters, he was free in spirit. In the end of
{541}
Acts 9 we see how he moved in support, anticipatively, of what God was about to introduce through the great servant He had just brought into view at Damascus, who preached the Son of God in the synagogue. The great economy we are brought into as amongst the gentiles, set up in assemblies -- not simply in one assembly as at Jerusalem, but in assemblies; that was already in view and the apostle anticipated it. Hence he goes down to Joppa and says to Aeneas, "rise up, and make thy couch for thyself" -- that was the sort of thing that was coming in -- Aeneas was to be ready for it. Then he raises Tabitha and presents her to the saints and widows alive -- another thought; that means that the new economy should be marked by a sense of local responsibility and by a living state of things in each locality.
Well now, that is Peter, and chapter 10 opens up to us how nobly he comes under the direction of heaven, for no servant is worthy of the name who is not amenable to the influence of heaven; the great bodies up there are emblematic of the power that is there spiritually for good, especially guidance. So Peter is amenable, and by his mouth, he says, the gentiles -- that is, we ourselves in our forefathers -- heard the gospel. Surely we can but speak well of one through whom such light is given to us! He justified the whole matter, too, in Jerusalem, and then, as I said, in Jerusalem, according to chapter 15, Peter with James and John gave to Paul and Barnabas the right hands of fellowship -- a very unifying thought. The work of God is now to move on in unity and happy sympathy; the great leaders being of one mind. So he came down to Antioch, apparently in this frame of mind, but as certain came from James; that is, the influence of Jerusalem was transmitted through some sinister agency, for
{542}
it was not simply a word, but certain persons under James's influence came and influenced Peter.
Now that is what is in mind in these verses, so that an issue arose in regard to straightforwardness or uprightness; the Spirit of God introducing it at this juncture, in relation to the issue at Galatia which is constantly occurring amongst the people of God, to warn us against any element other than uprightness, any opaqueness, any want of transparency, any want of brotherly simplicity and confidence between those who serve, and between the brethren generally. The apostle says, "when I saw that they do not walk straightforwardly ...". It was a very real issue, the truth of the gospel was involved, but it was a moral issue, too, this matter of uprightness. For a man to come out and say something wrong, is one thing, but to be underhanded about it, to be inconsistent in it, to be manifestly darkened by some adverse influence in it, is another thing -- that involves what is moral, a state of soul. So it is introduced, as I said, in this great issue, by the apostle Paul, and he contends with Peter "before them all". It is an open matter now, he had already said things privately at Jerusalem, lest he might "run or had run in vain", for it is not of God to bring great servants into discredit publicly -- surely not; but when there is a doctrinal issue of this kind, we had better speak publicly, we had better speak out -- and that is what he did. He did not take Peter aside at this time -- he did at Jerusalem under other circumstances, and so did Aquila and Priscilla in regard of Apollos, they "took him to them" -- but this time it is a public matter; all the saints are involved, and all the saints are to understand what is at issue and are to judge the whole matter. For, after all, christianity is not only in the leaders, let that be understood. Christianity is in the saints, it lies in the assembly; it is what
{543}
we are in relation to one another that affords opportunity for God to work out the great thoughts that He has in mind in christianity; it involves every one of us, and so the saints are to understand. If there be an issue so serious, it is well the saints should understand; and so the apostle does not shrink from speaking to Peter before them all, from rebuking before all, his distinguished fellow-servant, his elder brother, as I might say -- "he was to be condemned".
Of course, as in the case of David, the more distinguished a man is, the more serious is any defect or any sin in him; here Peter was to be blamed, there was no doubt about it. Whatever people might say in regard of the apparent indiscretion of a younger man rebuking an older one, the matter was too serious to admit of anything else; it was a matter of the want of uprightness; for Peter at heart knew the truth and ultimately stood by it; as the sequel shows, he loved and valued Paul, but for the moment he came under the influence of others. The most spiritual of us is exposed to that, and there is nothing more subtle than influence of this kind coming from another. With the very best intent I just listen to a brother: why should I not believe what he says? Well, why should I, if it involves another brother? why should I, if it is a question of the truth? Even in Paul's own case, the Bereans, we are told, "searched the scriptures daily, whether those things were so". So that the point is to be *sure* about things, not just to take them for granted; because one witness is not enough in any matter, it is in the mouth of two or three witnesses that every word is established. However distinguished a brother may be, his word is not enough if it involves another brother or brothers, and in this case it involved the truth, and so strong was the influence from Jerusalem that even Barnabas, Paul's fellow-worker
{544}
among the gentiles, was carried away; it was a question of dissimulation.
The doctrine, of course, was the most serious thing, but think of such men coming under the influence of a vicious wind of dissimulation and carried away by it! That is all I have to say about that, it is only to bring out the truth that is in mind, namely the truth of uprightness, how it leads up to a moral victory, also a victory for the truth.
The facts show what one man can do even against odds. The combination against the apostle Paul was strong at that time, but eventually his faithfulness gained the day. How the apostle Peter came back to Paul is very beautiful; I suppose he never spoke so well about him as he did when he was about to put off his tabernacle. It is well for us who are older in the service to remember that the time is drawing nearer when we must put off our tabernacles, unless the Lord comes. It is well to have our eyes on the younger men, and not to discredit them in any way. If we are about to breathe our last we shall be glad surely to look around and see that the ground will be held. The best is said about the apostle Paul when Peter is about to put off his tabernacle, and he places his writings on the level of the Holy Scriptures; he could hardly honour him more. That suffices in regard of Peter. One would like to leave with the brethren, especially the young ones here, a right impression of the great apostle, and how the work of God brought out in him at the end perfect consonance with the testimony of the apostle Paul.
Now I go on to David to show how this matter of Jasher, of uprightness, appeared in him. I have read from a section in his history which was transitional, and it is these transitional periods that are most testing, for in regard of a brother today there may be still a question as to whether he is ready
{545}
to come forward into prominence, and he is apt to take short cuts which will always be against him in time. The whole road to the desired result has to be travelled from this point of view; distinction in the service of God is reached by a road which has to be travelled. Not that this should ever be a goal in the eyes of a servant, but it is apt to be, in young men particularly, and in transitional periods, that is, when their position in the testimony is still uncertain. Things, in the government of God, have happened and may cause us elation, if they are in our favour; they may cause us to make unseemly remarks seeing that the circumstances may involve humiliation in others, disaster in others. Indeed, it may be in those who have served, but now have ceased to do so, and we are so likely, in these transitional periods, to make statements and take up attitudes that are unseemly, and indeed to overlook what the persons have been in the service of God; namely, that they have been really anointed. If a man has ever been anointed and served well, that is to be remembered and never to be forgotten whatever his subsequent history, whatever his end; what he has been as anointed of God can never be forgotten, nor will any one who is upright, whose name or composition is worthy of the book of Jasher, make little of one whom God has used.
This is how matters stand in the passages I have read. Saul had been slain on the mountains of Gilboa -- one of the most sorrowful parts of Israel's history! How will David take the news? He is the ostensible king now, but then, you never can be sure in such circumstances; our calculations often fail us. Not that it could be so in this case, for David was king according to divine choice, but I am making a *present* application, for our calculations do not always mature. Now as the tidings come of Saul's disaster, David is rightly affected. I want every one
{546}
here -- including myself -- to drink into this lesson how *rightly* affected he is. It meant a change for the better in his whole life, it meant unquestionably the kingdom for him; but he is not thinking of that, he is thinking of God's servant that had been. Think of such a man as Jonathan slain in his high places! "The beauty of Israel is slain upon thy high places". "Saul and Jonathan, beloved and pleasant in their lives, Even in their death were not divided". In truth David must have had real enjoyment with these men in earlier days; we know he had with Jonathan -- his love surpassed all human loves to him, and he associated him with his father. How could he be complacent in such circumstances? How could any true lover of Christ be complacent in such a disaster? For in truth the enemy has had a hand in it, and it has brought a once-valued man, a once-distinguished man, once anointed with oil as king over God's people, into a disaster -- slain by uncircumcised hands. The very messenger who brought the news has to die! Such is David's feeling, and he is a real man; no feigned feelings with David, he was genuine, he felt the thing, and hence this lament of his.
I speak thus, just to bring out what is becoming in us, as, it may be, on the road to distinction in the service of God, or, I should say, perhaps, more correctly, on the road to more usefulness -- for that was exactly the case here -- on the road to serving the people of God better than they had been served. So it may be that many of us are in such a transitional state of things, and the outlook is that the saints need help, they need leaders, they need service and rule. David is brought in to show his feelings here in song; "the song of the bow", I suppose, is the proper title of it -- and all that accompanied it, both in this chapter and later, in regard to Ishbosheth and Abner. In these three
{547}
